Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Ghasb of Land
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for usurped land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on liability for usurped land, distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, and evaluate the evidence for each position.

Core Idea
Land cannot be the subject of ghasb; the usurper is not liable for its value if it perishes.

This lesson examines a classical intra-Hanafi dispute over the nature of ghasb (usurpation) of immovable property. While Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that land is not susceptible to ghasb and thus no liability for its value, Imam Muhammad argues for full liability. The disagreement rests on differing interpretations of prophetic hadith and the underlying concept of movability in ghasb. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply Hanafi legal theory to property rights and liability.

Guided Path
1
State the factual scenario
Identify the act (ghasb of land) and the event (land perishes in usurper's hand). Note that the dispute is about liability for the land itself, not for any damage caused to it.
The phrase 'فهلک في يده' (perished in his hand) is key: it means the land was destroyed or otherwise rendered useless.
2
Identify the two positions
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: no liability (لم يضمنه). Imam Muhammad: liable (يضمنه). Determine which imam is the majority within the school.
The source states 'عند ابي حنيفة وابي يوسف' – the majority position of the two leading imams.
3
Analyze the proof for Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf
Their proof is the hadith about reviving dead land, which implies that only movable property can be misappropriated. Land is not movable, thus ghasb rules do not apply to it.
This is a dalil naqli (textual proof) from the Sunnah.
4
Analyze Imam Muhammad's proof
His proof is the hadith about taking a span of land unjustly. This hadith indicates that taking land is a great sin, implying it can be misappropriated and thus subject to liability.
The opposing school (within Hanafi) uses the same category of proof but different hadith.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if land perishes, the usurper pays nothing according to the mufta bihi position, or pays the value according to Imam Muhammad. This affects financial liability in property disputes.
The mufta bihi is the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, supported by al-Marghinani.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٥) واذا غصب عقارا فهلک في يده لم يضمنه عند ابي حنيفة وابي يوسف رحمهما الله وقال محمد يضمنه.
(5) And if he usurped land and it perished in his hand, he is not liable according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on them, and Muhammad said: he is liable.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding ghasb of immovable property (aqar). Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that land cannot be the subject of ghasb because it is not movable; thus if the land perishes in the usurper's hand, he is not liable for its value. Their proof is the hadith: 'Whoever revives dead land, it is his, and there is no right for a wrongdoer's root.' This indicates that the wrongdoer (ghasib) has no right, but also no liability for the land itself. Imam Muhammad holds that land can be subject to ghasb because the usurper takes possession and excludes the owner. His proof is the hadith: 'Whoever takes a span of land unjustly will be sunk into seven earths on the Day of Judgment.' This shows that taking land is a sin and thus subject to ghasb rules. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, as it is the majority view and consistent with the definition of ghasb requiring movability.

Key Terms
عقار (aqar): Immovable property, especially land and buildings.
ضمان (daman): Liability, guarantee, financial responsibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, if someone usurps a piece of land and the land is destroyed, what is the ruling?
A) He must pay the value of the land.
B) He is not liable for the land.
C) He must return a similar piece of land.
D) He must pay half the value.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that land cannot be the object of ghasb, so the usurper is not liable for its value if it perishes; this is the majority view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Liability for Damage by Usurper's Act or Dwelling
Ruling on compensation for damage by usurper's actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on liability for damage caused by a usurper's act or dwelling and explain the basis for the Hanafi consensus.

Core Idea
A usurper is liable for any damage he causes, even by dwelling, according to all Hanafi imams.

The text presents a clear ruling from the Hanafi school: when a usurper (ghasib) causes any deficiency (naqs) to the property he has wrongfully taken, whether through a direct act or simply by dwelling there, he must compensate for that damage. This principle is agreed upon by Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, making it a foundational rule in the law of ghasb.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wrongful act and the consequential damage
Recognize that the act is ghasb (usurpation) and that the damage is the deficiency (naqs) that occurs to the property.
Ghasb is defined as wrongful possession of another's property without right.
2
Determine the ruling for the usurper's liability
The text states that the usurper 'must compensate' (damana) for any deficiency caused by his act or dwelling.
Compensation is wajib (obligatory) in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Note the consensus among the three imams
The phrase 'in the opinion of all of them' indicates unanimity among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad.
Such consensus within the school is a strong source of law.
4
Understand the legal basis for liability
The liability arises because the usurper directly caused the damage. This is supported by the hadith where the Prophet (ﷺ) replaced a broken bowl with a sound one.
Direct causation (ta'di bil-fi'l) is a key principle in Hanafi tort law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٦) وما نقص منه بفعله او سكناه ضمنه في قولهم جميعا.
(6) And whatever deficiency occurs from his act or his dwelling, he is liable for it in the opinion of all of them.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This is a ruling statement covering compensation for damage to the usurped property (even if land) caused by the usurper's actions. All three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agree that if the usurper causes a deficiency—for example, by breaking a wall or damaging a building through dwelling—he must compensate for that damage. This is based on the hadith where the Prophet replaced a broken bowl with a sound one. The principle is that the usurper is responsible for any harm he directly causes.

Key Terms
نقص (naqs): Deficiency or damage to property, reduction in value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a usurper's dwelling causes damage to the land he usurped?
A) He is not liable because land is not subject to ghasb.
B) He is liable for the damage caused.
C) He must return the land only.
D) He pays double the damage.

Key Takeaway
A usurper must compensate for any deficiency in the property caused by his act or dwelling, according to all Hanafi imams.

Usurper's Liability for Destruction and Deficiency
Two short rulings on strict liability in usurpation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two rulings on usurper's liability for destruction and deficiency, and apply them to determine liability in any scenario where the property perishes or is damaged in the usurper's hand.

Core Idea
The usurper is liable for any loss or damage, by his act or not.

The text presents two fundamental rulings on the liability of a usurper (ghasib). If the usurped property is completely destroyed (halaka) or partially damaged (naqaṣa) while in his possession, he must compensate the owner. This is a strict liability rule that applies whether the damage was caused by his own act or by an external factor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first ruling
The text states: 'If the usurped property perishes in the hand of the usurper, by his act or without his act, then upon him is its guarantee.' Note the condition: the item is in the usurper's hand and it perishes. The ruling: the usurper is liable for full value.
2
Identify the second ruling
The text states: 'If it is deficient in his hand, then upon him is its guarantee.' Condition: the item suffers a deficiency (partial damage). Ruling: the usurper is liable for the amount of deficiency.
3
Summarize the principle
The usurper is a guarantor (damin) of the property; his wrongful possession shifts all risk to him. The ruling is absolute: no exception for cause, and applies to both total loss and partial damage.
4
Apply to a scenario
Consider a usurped car that is destroyed by a storm while in the usurper's garage: the usurper must pay the full value. If a usurped laptop gets a scratch, the usurper must pay the repair cost. In both cases, liability follows regardless of who caused the damage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٧) واذا هلک المغصوب فى يد الغاصب بفعله او بغیر فعله فعليه ضمانه (۸) وان نقص في يده فعليه ضمانه.
(7) And if the usurped property perishes in the hand of the usurper, by his act or without his act, then upon him is its guarantee. (8) And if it is deficient in his hand, then upon him is its guarantee.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
These are two related rulings from the matn. First, if the usurped property is destroyed (halaka) while in the usurper's hand, whether he caused it or not, he is liable for its value. Second, if it becomes deficient (naqasa) while in his hand, he is liable for the amount of deficiency. The underlying principle is that the usurper holds the property as a guarantor (damin). The hadith of the broken bowl from Aisha is cited as proof: when the bowl was broken, the Prophet replaced it with a sound one, showing that the person in whose custody the item was is responsible even if he did not break it. Thus, the usurper's liability is comprehensive: he is responsible for any loss or damage that occurs during his wrongful possession.

Key Terms
هلک (halaka): Complete destruction or perishing of the item.
نقص (naqs): Deficiency or partial damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a usurped item is destroyed by a third party while in the usurper's hand, who is liable under the Hanafi ruling?
A) The third party only.
B) The usurper is liable.
C) Neither, it is an act of God.
D) The owner bears the loss.

Key Takeaway
The usurper is absolutely liable for any loss or damage to the usurped property while it is in his possession.

Liability for Diminution by Usurper's Act or Dwelling
Ruling on liability for diminution caused by usurper's act or dwelling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the usurper's liability for diminution caused by his act or dwelling and explain the proof from the hadith of the cup.

Core Idea
The usurper is liable for any diminution, whether caused by his action or mere dwelling.

This lesson examines a key ruling in the law of usurpation (ghasp). The Hanafi school holds the usurper strictly liable for any diminution (naqs) that occurs to the usurped property, whether caused by his direct act or simply by his occupancy. The ruling is based on a clear analogy from the Prophet's (saw) own conduct when he broke a cup and replaced it with a sound one. Understanding this principle helps clarify the extent of the usurper's responsibility and the basis for compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is a usurper (ghasib) occupying or using another's property. The ruling: the usurper is liable (damān) for any diminution (naqs) caused by his act or dwelling.
The Urdu commentary specifically mentions land and house.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof cited is the hadith from Bukhari where the Prophet (saw) broke a cup and gave a sound one in exchange, keeping the broken one. This establishes that a person in wrongful possession must compensate for damage.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but accepted in fiqh.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The analogy: The cup was in the Prophet's possession (though not usurped), and he compensated for damage. By stronger reason, a usurper who deliberately takes property must compensate for any damage or diminution, whether by direct act or merely by dwelling (which causes wear and tear or market value loss).
The wajh al-dalalah is based on the general principle that any damage caused by the wrongful possessor must be compensated.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Within the Hanafi school, all three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agree. However, some other schools may restrict liability to active damage and not mere dwelling without negligence.
The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) because it is based on rigorous qiyas and the need to protect property rights. The hadith provides a clear precedent.
Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah affirms this ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[1556] (6) وما نقص منه بفعله او سكناه ضمنه في قولهم جميعا [1556) (6) اور جو نقص آ جائے غاصب کے فعل سے اور اس کی رہائش سے تو سب کے قول میں وہ ضامن ہوگا۔ غاصب کے فعل کی وجہ سے یا غاصب کے رہنے کی وجہ سے زمین میں نقص آ گیا تو اس نقص کا ضمان تینوں اماموں کے نزدیک غاصب لازم ہوگا۔ نقص کیا ہے اس لئے اس کا ضمان لازم ہو گا (2) او پر حدیث میں گزرا کہ پیالہ توڑ دیا تو اس کے بدلے میں صحیح پیالہ آپ نے دیا۔ عن انس ... فدفع القصعة الصحيحة وحبس المكسورة (الف) ( بخاری شریف ، باب اذا کسر قصعته او شیئالغیر ص ۳۳۷ نمبر ۲۴۸۱) جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ زمین یا گھر میں جو نقصان ہوا ہو اس کا ضمان غاصب پر لازم ہوگا۔
And whatever is diminished from it by his act or his dwelling, he is liable for it according to all of them. [Urdu:] And whatever loss occurs due to the usurper's act or his dwelling, then according to all (imams) he will be liable. If loss occurs to land due to the usurper's act or his residing, then the liability for that loss is obligatory upon the usurper according to the three imams. What is loss? Therefore liability becomes obligatory. And above in the hadith it passed that when [the Prophet] broke a cup, he gave a sound cup in exchange and kept the broken one. (Bukhari, Bab idha kasara qas'atahu aw shay'an, p.337 no.2481). From this it is known that for any damage occurring to land or house, the usurper is liable.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on usurpation (ghasp) regarding liability for diminution (naqs). The author states that the usurper (ghasib) is liable for any decrease in value or physical damage caused to the usurped property by his own act (fi'l) or even by his dwelling (sukna) — i.e., occupancy. The phrase 'fi qawlihim jami'an' indicates consensus among the three Hanafi masters: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The Urdu commentary specifies that this applies to immovable property like land and houses. The proof is derived from the hadith of Anas (ra) where the Prophet (saw) broke a cup and gave a sound one in compensation, establishing that any damage caused by one in possession of another's property incurs liability. This ruling is foundational in Hanafi fiqh for ghasp.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasp): The act of taking another's property wrongfully and without their consent, with the usurper exercising control.
ضمان (daman): Legal liability to compensate for loss or damage to another's property.
نقص (naqs): Diminution in value or physical integrity of the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the scope of the usurper's liability for diminution?
A) Only for damage caused by his direct act
B) Only for damage caused by his dwelling
C) For damage caused by his act or his dwelling
D) Only if the property is completely destroyed

Key Takeaway
The usurper is strictly liable for any diminution in the usurped property, whether from his direct act or from his mere dwelling, as established by the hadith of the cup.

Liability if Usurped Property Perishes
Ruling with proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when usurped property perishes and identify the proof from the hadith of the cup.

Core Idea
The usurper is liable for the property's destruction regardless of who caused it.

This passage from Al-Sharh al-Sameri clarifies a key principle of liability in ghasp (usurpation). When a usurper takes property, he becomes its guarantor; if it perishes, he must compensate the owner even if the destruction was caused by a third party. The ruling is supported by a well-known hadith of the Prophet (saw) involving a broken cup.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text states that if usurped property perishes in the usurper's hand — whether by his act or not — he is liable.
The act is ghasp (usurpation), and the ruling is wujub al-daman (obligation of compensation).
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith of the cup (حديث القصعة) as proof.
The Prophet (saw) compensated for a cup broken by 'Aisha while it was in his trust.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's compensation establishes that the custodian is liable for destruction of entrusted property, regardless of who caused it.
This is analogized to the usurper, who holds the property unlawfully.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some other schools may differentiate if the usurper was not negligent. The Hanafi school holds strict liability.
The hadith supports the Hanafi position.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi and consistent across Zahir al-Riwayah.
No intra-Hanafi dispute is recorded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[1557] (7) واذا هلک المغصوب فى يد الغاصب بفعله او بغير فعله فعليه ضمانه [1557] (7) اگر ہلاک ہو جائے مغصوب چیز غاصب کے ہاتھ میں غاصب کے فعل سے یا بغیر اس کے فعل سے تو اس کے او پر اس کا ضمان ہے۔ غاصب کے قبضے میں مغصوب چیز تھی اور اس دوران مغصوب چیز غاصب کی حرکت کی وجہ سے یا کسی اور کی حرکت کی وجہ سے ہلاک ہوگئی تو غاصب پر اس کا ضمان لازم ہوگا۔ چونکہ مغصوب چیز غاصب کے حوالے ہے اس لئے چاہے وہ ہلاک کرے یا اس کے قبضے میں رہتے ہوئے کسی اور نے ہلاک کی ، دونوں صورتوں میں غاصب ہی ضمان کا ذمہ دار ہو گا (2) پیالے والی حدیث میں حضرت عائشہ نے پیالہ توڑا تھا لیکن حضور نے اس کا بدلہ دیا۔ کیونکہ حضور کی ذمہ داری میں پیالہ تھا۔ جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ غاصب کی ذمہ داری میں کوئی بھی ہلاک کرے تو غاصب ہی ضمان کا ذمہ دار ہوگا۔
And if the usurped property perishes in the hand of the usurper by his act or not by his act, then upon him is its liability. [Urdu:] If the usurped property perishes in the hand of the usurper due to his action or due to someone else's action, then upon him is its liability. Since the usurped item is in the usurper's custody, whether he destroys it or someone else destroys it while in his possession, in both cases the usurper is the one responsible for liability. (2) In the hadith of the cup, Aisha (ra) broke the cup, but the Prophet (saw) gave its compensation because the cup was under his responsibility. From this it is known that if anyone destroys an item under the usurper's responsibility, the usurper is liable.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This ruling clarifies that once the usurper has taken possession, he becomes completely liable for the property's safety. If it perishes — whether by his direct action (like breaking) or by an external cause (like fire, or another person destroying it) — he must compensate the owner. The Urdu commentary explains that the usurper is akin to a guarantor; the hadith of the cup (where the Prophet compensated for a cup broken by Aisha while in his custody) supports this principle. The ruling is unanimous among the Hanafi masters.

Key Terms
هلاك (halak): Total destruction or loss of the property, making it unusable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the usurped property is destroyed by a stranger while in the usurper's hand, who is liable to the owner?
A) The stranger who destroyed it
B) The usurper
C) Both the usurper and the stranger equally
D) No one, since it was an accident

Key Takeaway
The usurper is liable for the destruction of usurped property irrespective of who caused it, based on the hadith of the cup.

Liability for Diminution While in Usurper's Possession
Ruling on liability for diminution of property in usurper's hand

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for partial damage to usurped property and explain the proof from the hadith of the cup.

Core Idea
A usurper is liable for any diminution of the property in his possession, even if slight.

This lesson builds on the previous rulings on total destruction. Here we consider cases where the property is not destroyed but suffers a loss in value or quality. The Hanafi school holds the usurper strictly liable for this diminution, drawing on the hadith of the cup. Let us analyze the precise ruling and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case
The property under usurpation has suffered a diminution (nuqsan) — partial damage or loss of value, not total destruction.
The Urdu commentary clarifies: 'magsub chiz halaak to nahi hui, albattah us mein kuch nuqsan ho gaya'.
2
State the ruling
The ruling is: 'عليه ضمان النقصان' — the usurper must compensate for the amount of diminution. This is a wajib obligation.
3
Recall the proof (dalil)
The proof is the hadith of the cup (al-qadah), where the Prophet ﷺ ordered compensation for a broken cup, which is a partial loss (the cup was damaged, not completely destroyed).
The text says this hadith 'has passed above', indicating it was cited earlier.
4
Understand the legal implication
The hadith shows that liability for diminution does not require proof of causation or negligence; the mere fact of possession and loss triggers the duty to compensate.
This is strict liability specific to usurpation (ghasb), distinct from trust (amanah).
5
Note the ikhtilaf
Some other schools require that the usurper actually caused the diminution. In the Hanafi view, the usurper is liable even if the loss occurred without his direct act, because the property was in his unlawful custody.
The practical difference: in Hanafi, if a third party damages the property while in the usurper's hands, the usurper still owes the owner, then can recover from the third party.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[1558] (8) وان نقص في يده فعليه ضمان النقصان [1558] (8) اور اگر اس کے قبضے میں نقصان ہو جائے تو اس پر نقصان کا ضمان ہوگا۔ غاصب کے قبضے میں مغصوب چیز تھی تو اس دوران مغصوب چیز ہلاک تو نہیں ہوئی البتہ اس میں کچھ نقصان ہو گیا تو نقصان کا ضمان غاصب پر لازم ہوگا۔ اوپر گزر چکی ہے اور پیالے والی حدیث بھی اوپر گزرگئی۔
And if it diminishes in his hand, then upon him is the liability for the diminution. [Urdu:] And if the property in his possession suffers a loss (but not complete destruction), then liability for that loss is upon the usurper. This has passed above, and the hadith of the cup has also passed above.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This ruling reiterates the principle that the usurper is liable for any decrease in value or damage short of total destruction. The Urdu commentary notes that it refers to cases where the property is not completely destroyed but has suffered some defect. The proof is the same hadith of the cup, where the Prophet compensated for the broken cup (which was damaged but not necessarily utterly destroyed — the broken pieces were kept). The ruling is straightforward and builds on the previous two.

Key Terms
نقصان (nuqsan): Diminution or damage to the property, not amounting to total loss.
ضمان (daman): Liability; in this context, the obligation to compensate for the loss.
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation; unlawful possession of another's property without right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if the usurped property is not destroyed but only damaged?
A) No liability because it still exists
B) The usurper is liable only if he was negligent
C) The usurper is liable for the amount of diminution
D) The usurper must replace the entire property

Key Takeaway
The usurper is liable for any diminution in the value of the property while in his possession, based on the hadith of the cup.

The Owner's Option When a Sheep Is Slaughtered Without Permission
Ruling on the owner's option when a sheep is slaughtered without permission, with reasoning based on the dual status of the slaughtered animal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two remedies available to the owner when someone slaughters his sheep without permission and identify the legal reasoning behind the option.

Core Idea
A slaughtered sheep is neither fully destroyed nor intact; the owner may choose full value or the diminution.

This passage from the Hanafi text addresses a subtle case in ghasp (usurpation). When someone slaughters another's sheep without permission, the sheep undergoes a transformation: it is dead, but its meat is edible. The owner is not forced to accept one outcome but has a choice (khiyar) between treating the act as total destruction or partial damage. This ruling reflects the Hanafi school's attention to preserving the owner's interest and the nuanced status of the property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the parties
Recognize that the act is slaughtering another's sheep without permission. The parties are the owner and the slaughterer (usurper).
2
Recognize the owner's right
The owner has a khiyar (option) between two remedies, not a single fixed remedy.
3
Describe the first remedy: Full value
The owner can demand the full market value of the sheep and must surrender the carcass to the slaughterer.
This treats the act as total destruction (halak).
4
Describe the second remedy: Diminution
The owner can keep the meat and demand only the difference between the value before and after slaughter.
This treats the act as partial damage (naqs).
5
Apply the principle to non-edible animals
If the animal is non-edible (ghayr ma'kul al-lahm), slaughter renders it worthless, so only the full-value remedy applies.
The carcass has no value, so the option collapses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[1559] (9) ومن ذبح شاة غيره بغير امره فمالكها بالخيار ان شاء ضمنه قيمتها وسلمها اليه وان شاء ضمنه نقصانها [1559) (9) کسی نے دوسرے کی بکری بغیر اس کے حکم کے ذبح کردی تو بکری کے مالک کو اختیار ہے چاہے تو بکری کی قیمت کا ضامن بنادے اور بکری ذبح کرنے والے کو سپرد کر دے۔ اور چاہے تو اس کے نقصان کا ضامن بنادے۔ بکری ذبح کرنے کے بعد اس کی دو حیثیتیں ہو جاتی ہیں۔ ایک اعتبار سے وہ ہلاک ہوگئی کیونکہ وہ زندہ نہیں رہی۔ اور دوسرے اعتبار سے اس کا گوشت کھانے کے قابل ہے اس لئے مکمل ہلاک نہیں ہوئی۔ بلکہ اس میں نقصان ہوا۔ اس لئے مالک کو دو اختیار ہوں گے چاہے تو بکری کو ہلاک شمار کر کے پوری بکری کی قیمت غاصب سے وصول کرے اور ذبح شدہ بکری غاصب کو دیدے۔ اور دوسری صورت یہ ہے کہ بکری کا گوشت رکھ لے اور زندہ اور ذبح شدہ بکری کی قیمت میں جو فرق ہے وہ ذبح کرنے والے سے وصول کرے۔ اور اگر جانور غیر ماکول اللحم ہو اور ذبح کے بعد کسی قیمت کے نہ رہے تو وہ ہلاک شمار کیا جائے گا اور غاصب کو پوری قیمت ادا کرنی ہوگی۔
And whoever slaughters another's sheep without his permission, then its owner has an option: if he wishes, he may hold him liable for its value and surrender it to him; and if he wishes, he may hold him liable for its diminution. [Urdu:] If someone slaughters another's sheep without his permission, the owner has the choice: either he can make the slaughterer liable for the full price and hand over the slaughtered sheep to him, or he can make him liable for the diminution. After slaughter, the sheep has two aspects: in one sense it is destroyed because it is no longer alive; in another sense its meat is edible, so it is not completely destroyed but has suffered a loss. So the owner has two options: either treat it as destroyed and collect the full value from the usurper and give him the slaughtered sheep, or keep the meat and collect the difference between the value of a live sheep and the slaughtered one from the slaughterer. And if the animal is not edible (ghayr ma'kul al-lahm) and after slaughter it has no value, then it will be considered destroyed and the usurper must pay full value.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage introduces a nuanced ruling in ghasp: when someone slaughters another's sheep without permission, the sheep is in a middle state — not completely destroyed (since meat remains) but no longer alive. The owner is given a choice (khiyar). Option 1: treat it as total destruction (halak) and demand full market value from the slaughterer, while handing over the carcass to him. Option 2: treat it as a diminution (naqs) and keep the meat, claiming only the difference between the sheep's value before and after slaughter. The Urdu commentary adds a qualification: if the animal is non-edible (e.g., donkey), slaughter renders it worthless, so it must be treated as total destruction, and full value is due. The proof is derived from general principles of liability for usurped property, though no specific hadith is quoted here.

Key Terms
خيار (khiyar): The legal right of the owner to choose between two remedies: full value or compensation for diminution.
ذبح (dhabh): Ritual slaughter, which renders an animal dead but potentially edible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone slaughters another's sheep without permission, what can the owner choose?
A) Only the full value of the sheep
B) Only the diminution in value
C) Either the full value (and give the carcass) or the diminution (and keep the carcass)
D) The owner has no choice; the slaughterer must pay full value

Key Takeaway
The owner of a slaughtered sheep has the option to claim either full value (and surrender the carcass) or the diminution (and keep the meat), reflecting the Hanafi recognition of the property's residual value.

Liability for a Slight Tear in Another's Garment
Ruling on liability for a small tear in another's garment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for a slight tear in another's garment and distinguish it from total destruction.

Core Idea
A slight tear incurs liability only for the reduction in value, not full replacement.

This brief ruling from a Hanafi fiqh text clarifies the extent of liability when one damages another's property partially. Unlike total destruction, a partial damage that does not destroy utility gives rise to compensation for the loss in value only. Understanding this distinction is essential for applying principles of tort liability in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal consequence
The act is tearing another's garment. The consequence is liability (daman) for the diminution (naqs).
2
Distinguish the degree of damage
The tear is slight (kharq yasir), meaning the garment remains usable. This is key: if the tear were excessive (kharq kathir) and destroyed utility, full price would be due.
3
Determine the measure of liability
Liability is limited to the decrease in value caused by the tear. The owner can claim compensation only for that loss, not the full garment price.
Principle: al-daman bi qadr al-naqs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[1560] (10) ومن خرق ثوب غیرہ خرقا يسيرا ضمن نقصانه [1560] (10) کسی نے دوسرے کا کپڑا تھوڑا سا پھاڑا تو اس کے نقصان کا ضامن ہوگا۔ چونکہ تھوڑا سا پھاڑا ہے اس لئے وہ ابھی قابل استفادہ ہے اس لئے کپڑا مکمل ہلاک نہیں ہوا۔ بلکہ اس میں نقصان ہوا۔ اس لئے مالک پھاڑنے والے سے نقصان کا ضمان لے سکتا ہے۔
And whoever tears another's garment slightly, he is liable for its diminution. [Urdu:] If someone tears another's cloth a little, he is liable for its loss. Since it is a small tear, it is still usable, so the cloth is not completely destroyed but has suffered loss. Therefore the owner can claim compensation for the loss from the tearer.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This ruling distinguishes between slight damage and total destruction. A small tear does not render the garment completely useless but reduces its value. The usurper (or the person who tears it) is liable only for the difference in value before and after the tear, not the full price. This is consistent with the general principle that liability for diminution (naqs) applies when the property retains some utility.

Key Terms
خرق يسير (kharq yasir): A slight tear that does not completely ruin the garment's utility.
ضمن نقصانه (daman nuqsanihi): Liability for its diminution; compensation equal to the decrease in value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone makes a small tear in another's shirt, what must they compensate?
A) The full value of the shirt
B) Only the decrease in value caused by the tear
C) Nothing, because it is still wearable
D) A new shirt of similar quality

Key Takeaway
A slight tear in another's garment incurs liability only for the reduction in value, not full replacement.

Liability for Excessive Tear (Kharq Kathir) – Full Value Claim
Ruling on liability for excessive tear

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a garment is torn excessively and identify the legal basis for claiming full value.

Core Idea
An excessive tear that destroys utility is treated as total destruction, entitling the owner to full value.

In the previous lesson, we saw that a minor tear only entitles the owner to compensation for diminution. Here, we examine the opposite: when the tear is so severe that the garment becomes useless. Hanafi fiqh treats this as halak (destruction), and the owner may claim the full market value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of tear
Distinguish between kharq yasir (minor tear) and kharq kathir (excessive tear). The ruling depends on the extent of damage to the garment's utility.
This distinction is key for liability.
2
Understand the legal consequence of excessive tear
An excessive tear that destroys most benefits is legally considered halak (destruction), not mere damage. This triggers full value liability.
Halak is a legal status, not necessarily physical annihilation.
3
Apply the ruling
The owner may demand the full market value from the wrongdoer, as the garment is treated as if it perished entirely.
The wrongdoer must pay full price.
4
Distinguish from minor tear
Contrast with the previous ruling: for a minor tear, the owner only claims the diminution in value (arshi qisas). For excessive tear, full value is due.
The difference hinges on whether the garment retains utility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[1561] (11) وان خرق خرقا كثيرا يبطل عامة منافعه [1561] (11) اور اگر بہت زیادہ پھاڑ دیا جس سے اکثر منافع ختم ہو گئے تو اس کے مالک کے لئے جائز ہے کہ اس کی پوری قیمت کا ضامن بنادے۔ کپڑے کو اتنا زیادہ پھاڑا کہ وہ کسی کام کا نہیں رہا تو اب یہ نقصان نہیں ہے بلکہ کپڑے کی ہلاکت ہے اس لئے مالک کو اس کی پوری قیمت لینے کا اختیار ہے۔
And if he tears it excessively, destroying most of its benefits, then its owner may make him liable for its full value. [Urdu:] And if he tears it so much that most of its benefits are lost, then it is permissible for its owner to make him liable for its full value. If the cloth is torn so much that it becomes useless, then this is not mere diminution but destruction (halak), so the owner has the right to take full value.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This ruling is the counterpart to the previous one. When the damage is so severe that the garment loses its primary utility (e.g., torn into pieces, cannot be worn or mended), it is legally considered destroyed (halak). The owner may then demand the full market value from the wrongdoer. This is consistent with the earlier ruling on total destruction: the usurper is liable for full value if the property perishes in his hand.

Key Terms
خرق كثير (kharq kathir): An excessive tear that destroys most of the garment's benefit, amounting to total destruction.
هلاك (halak): Legal destruction: loss of utility even if physical object remains.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone tears a garment so badly that it loses most of its benefits, what can the owner claim?
A) Only the diminution in value
B) Nothing, since the garment still exists
C) The full value of the garment
D) Only the cost of repair

Key Takeaway
An excessive tear that destroys utility is legally treated as total destruction, entitling the owner to full compensation.

Owner's Options When His Sheep Is Slaughtered Without Permission
Ruling on usurper's liability for slaughtering another's sheep

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two options available to the owner when his sheep is slaughtered without permission and explain the rationale based on the dual nature of the slaughtered animal.

Core Idea
The owner may claim full value or depreciation because the slaughtered sheep is both destroyed and beneficial.

Imagine someone slaughters your sheep without asking. Legally, the sheep is both dead (destroyed) but its meat is still usable. This duality gives you a choice: treat it as fully gone and demand full value, or keep the meat and claim only the loss in value. This lesson explains the ruling and its proof from hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? Slaughtering another's sheep without permission. The ruling is that the owner has an option between two remedies.
This is a ruling from Kitab al-Ghasb (Book of Usurpation).
2
Analyze the dual nature of the slaughtered sheep
After slaughter, the sheep is dead (destruction) but its meat is edible (benefit). Thus it is both destroyed and not fully destroyed.
This duality is the key to understanding the two options.
3
Understand the two remedies
The owner can either claim the full value and surrender the sheep to the usurper, or keep the sheep and claim only the depreciation (difference between live and slaughtered value).
The choice belongs to the owner, not the usurper.
4
Examine the hadith proof
The hadith of Juraij: When his hermitage was demolished, the people rebuilt it exactly. This supports the principle of compensating for destruction with equivalent value.
The hadith is from Sahih al-Bukhari and shows that full compensation is due when property is destroyed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذبح شاة غيره بغير امره فمالكها بالخيار ان شاء ضمنه قيمتها وسلمها اليه وان شاء ضمنه نقصانها
كسي نے دوسرے كي بکری بغیر اس كے حکم كے ذبح كردی تو بکری كے مالك كو اختیار ہے چاہے تو بکری کی قیمت کا ضامن بنادے اور بکری ذبح کرنے والے کو سپرد کر دے۔ اور چاہے تو اس کے نقصان کا ضامن بنادے۔ بکری ذبح کرنے کے بعد اس کی دو حیثیتیں ہو جاتی ہیں۔ ایک اعتبار سے وہ ہلاک ہوگئی کیونکہ وہ زندہ نہیں رہی۔ اور دوسرے اعتبار سے اس کا گوشت کھانے کے قابل ہے اس لئے مکمل ہلاک نہیں ہوئی۔ بلکہ اس میں نقصان ہوا۔ اس لئے مالک کو دو اختیار ہوں گے چاہے تو بکری کو ہلاک شمار کر کے پوری بکری کی قیمت غاصب سے وصول کرے اور ذبح شدہ بکری غاصب کو دیدے۔ اور دوسری صورت یہ ہے کہ بکری کا گوشت رکھ لے اور زندہ اور ذبح شدہ بکری کی قمیت میں جو فرق ہے وہ ذبح کرنے والے سے وصول کرے۔
اں جہاں ہلاکت اور نقصان دونوں حیثیتیں ہوں وہاں دونوں کی رعایت کی جاسکتی ہے۔ اور اگر جانور غیر ماکول اللحم ہو اور ذبح کے بعد کسی قیمت کے نہ رہے تو وہ ہلاک شمار کیا جائے گا اور غاصب کو پوری قیمت ادا کرنی ہوگی۔
حدیث میں ہے کہ جریج کا گرجا توڑا تو لوگوں نے ان کا گر جانیا [؟] بنا دیا۔ حدیث کا ٹکڑا یہ ہے عن ابي هريرة قال قال رسول الله ه كان رجل في بني اسرائيل يقال له جريج ... فاتوه وكسروا صومعته فانزلوه وسبوه فتوضأ وصلى ثم اتى الغلام فقال من ابوک یا غلام ؟ قال الراعى قالوا نبنی صومعتك من ذهب ، قال لا الا من الطين (الف) ( بخاری شریف ، باب اذ اهدم حا کا فلیسبین مثلہ ص ۱۳۳۷ نمبر ۲۴۸۲)
اس حدیث میں گرجا منہدم کر دیا تو اس کے مثل بنا دیا۔ حان ظافر
حاشیہ : (الف) آپ نے فرمایا بنی اسرائیل میں ایک آدمی کا نام جریج تھا۔ لوگوں نے ان کا گرجا توڑ دیا اور ان کو نکالا اور گالیاں دی ۔ جریج نے وضو کیا اور نماز پڑھی پھر لڑکے کے پاس آکر پوچھا تمہارا باپ کون ہے؟ کہا چہ واہا۔ لوگوں نے کہا ہم لوگ سونے کا گرجا بنا دیں گے ۔ جریج نے کہا نہیں مٹی کا بنا دو۔
And whoever slaughters another's sheep without his permission, its owner has the option: if he wishes he can make him liable for its value and surrender it to him, and if he wishes he can make him liable for its depreciation. Someone slaughtered another's sheep without his permission: the owner of the sheep has the choice, if he wants he can hold him liable for the full value of the sheep and hand over the slaughtered sheep to the slaughterer. And if he wants he can hold him liable for its depreciation. After slaughtering the sheep, it has two aspects: from one aspect it is destroyed because it is no longer alive, and from another aspect its meat is edible so it is not completely destroyed, rather it suffered depreciation. Therefore the owner has two options: either consider the sheep destroyed and recover the full value from the usurper and give the slaughtered sheep to the usurper, or keep the meat and recover the difference between the value of the live and slaughtered sheep. Where both destruction and depreciation exist, both can be considered. And if the animal is non-edible meat and after slaughter it has no value, then it is considered destroyed and the usurper must pay the full value. In a hadith: The hermitage of Juraij was demolished, so the people rebuilt it for him. The hadith text: from Abu Hurairah ... they broke his hermitage, brought him down, and reviled him. He performed wudu and prayed, then came to the boy and asked, 'Who is your father, O boy?' He said, 'The shepherd.' They said, 'We will build your hermitage of gold.' He said, 'No, only of clay.' In this hadith, the hermitage was demolished and then rebuilt exactly.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Kitab al-Ghasb (Book of Usurpation). The ruling states that if a person slaughters someone else's sheep without permission, the owner has two options: he can either claim the full market value of the sheep and surrender the slaughtered animal to the wrongdoer, or he can keep the sheep and claim only the depreciation in value (the difference between its value before and after slaughter). The commentator explains that after slaughter, the sheep has two aspects: it is dead (destruction) but its meat is still beneficial (depreciation). Since both aspects exist, the owner may choose either remedy. If the animal is not edible (non-consumable meat) and thus worthless after slaughter, it is treated as fully destroyed, and the usurper must pay the full value. The hadith of Juraij is cited to support the principle of compensation for destruction: when Juraij's hermitage was demolished, it was rebuilt exactly as before, illustrating that compensation for destruction is for the exact equivalent. This hadith is from Sahih al-Bukhari.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): The wrongful taking and control of another's property without consent.
ضمان (daman): Financial liability; obligation to compensate for loss or damage.
خيار (khiyar): Option or choice granted to the owner to select between two remedies.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone slaughters another's sheep without permission, what options does the owner have?
A) The owner can only claim the full value of the sheep.
B) The owner can either claim full value and surrender the sheep, or keep the sheep and claim depreciation.
C) The owner must accept the slaughtered sheep and cannot claim anything.
D) The owner can claim double the value.

Key Takeaway
When an animal is slaughtered without permission, the owner may choose between full compensation or depreciation because the animal has a dual status of destruction and benefit.

Liability for Slight Damage: Defect Compensation
Ruling on liability for minor tear in another's cloth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on liability for a small tear in another's cloth and identify the conditions that determine whether full value or defect compensation is owed.

Core Idea
A slight tear that leaves cloth usable requires compensation for defect only, not full value.

You borrowed someone's cloak and accidentally tore a small corner. Is the cloth considered destroyed? The Hanafi ruling distinguishes between total loss and partial damage. In this lesson, we examine the liability for a minor tear—a case where the item remains functional but diminished in value. The compensation is proportional to the loss.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its classification
The act is tearing another's cloth. This is a tort (damage to property). Determine whether it is a small tear (yaseer) or a large tear.
The size of the tear is crucial.
2
Determine the extent of damage
If the tear is small enough that the cloth remains usable, it is considered partial damage. If the cloth becomes unusable, it is total destruction.
Usability is key.
3
Apply the ruling
For a small tear, the wrongdoer is liable only for the defect (nuqsan) – the decrease in market value. For a large tear, full value compensation is due.
The text specifies 'ضمن نقصانه' (liable for its defect).
4
Understand the basis
The cloth is still beneficial, so it is not completely destroyed. Liability is proportional to the actual loss.
The Urdu commentary explains: 'قابل استفادہ ہے' (it is still usable).
5
Note the practical consequence
The owner can claim the difference in market value before and after the tear, not the full replacement cost.
This prevents overcompensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن خرق ثوب غیرہ خرقا يسيرا ضمن نقصانه
كسي نے دوسرے کا کپڑا تھوڑا سا پھاڑا تو اس کے نقصان کا ضامن ہوگا۔ چونکہ تھوڑا سا پھاڑا ہے اس لئے وہ ابھی قابل استفادہ ہے اس لئے کپڑا مکمل ہلاک نہیں ہوا۔ بلکہ اس میں نقصان ہوا۔ اس لئے مالک پھاڑنے والے سے نقصان کا ضمان لے سکتا ہے۔
اور حدیث اوپر گزرگئی (بخاری شریف، نمبر۲۴۸۲) خرق : پھاڑا، پیرا : تھوڑا۔
And whoever tears another's cloth a small tear is liable for its defect. Someone tore another's cloth a little: he will be liable for its defect. Since he tore it a little, it is still usable, so the cloth is not completely destroyed, rather it suffered a defect. Therefore the owner can claim compensation for the defect from the tearer. And the hadith has already passed (al-Bukhari, no. 2482). Tear: rent; small: little.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This is a ruling on usurpation where the usurper tears another's cloth only slightly. Since the cloth remains usable despite the tear, it is not considered completely destroyed. The wrongdoer is liable only for the decrease in value (the defect), not the full value. The commentator notes that the hadith of Juraij (mentioned in the previous lesson) also supports the principle of compensating for loss, though here the loss is partial. This ruling is straightforward: compensation is proportional to the damage.

Key Terms
خرق (kharq): Tearing or ripping a fabric.
نقصان (nuqsan): Defect or decrease in value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone tears another's cloth only slightly, what is the liability?
A) He must pay the full value of the cloth.
B) He must pay compensation equal to the defect (loss in value).
C) He must replace the cloth with a new one.
D) He is not liable if the tear is very small.

Key Takeaway
A slight tear that leaves the cloth usable warrants only defect compensation, not full value.

Tearing Cloth Extensively: Full Value Due
Ruling on liability for extensive tear destroying most benefits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that an extensive tear destroying most benefits of cloth requires the wrongdoer to pay full value, and distinguish this from compensation for a minor defect.

Core Idea
If a tear destroys most benefits of cloth, the full value is due — it is destruction, not mere defect.

This passage completes the discussion on tearing cloth from the previous lesson. While a small tear only compensates the reduction in value, an extensive tear that renders the cloth unusable is treated as total destruction. The Hanafi school holds that when most benefits (manafi') are lost, the property is considered effectively destroyed, and the owner is entitled to the full market value. This distinction is based on the principle that loss of utility equates to loss of the property itself.

Guided Path
1
Assess the extent of the tear
Determine if the tear is minor or extensive. The text specifies 'a large tear' (خرقا كثيرا) that nullifies most benefits.
A small tear that leaves the cloth usable is a defect; a large tear that destroys most utility is destruction.
2
Apply the benefit-loss test
Check if the tear destroys 'عامة منافعه' (most of its benefits). This is the threshold for full liability.
The Urdu commentary clarifies: if the cloth becomes unusable (کسی کام کا نہیں رہا), it is destruction.
3
Classify the damage
Distinguish between defect (نقصان) and destruction (ہلاکت). A defect reduces value; destruction eliminates utility.
The commentator explicitly says: 'this is not a defect but destruction of the cloth.'
4
Determine compensation
If destruction: full value (جملة قيمته). If defect: compensation for the reduction in value only.
The ruling applies regardless of the cloth's original price.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان خرق خرقا كثيرا يبطل عامة منافعه فلمالكه ان يضمنه جميع قيمته
اور اگر بہت زیادہ پھاڑ دیا جس سے اکثر منافع ختم ہو گئے تو اس کے مالک کے لئے جائز ہے کہ اس کی پوری قیمت کا ضامن بنادے۔
کپڑے کو اتنا زیادہ پھاڑا کہ ہ کسی کام کا نہیں رہا تواب یہ نقصان نہیں ہے بلکہ کپڑے کی ہلاکت ہے اس لئے مالک کو اس کی پوری قیمت لینے کا اختیار ہے۔
And if he tears it a large tear that nullifies most of its benefits, then its owner may make him liable for its entire value. And if he tore it so much that it became unusable, then this is not a defect but destruction of the cloth, so the owner has the right to take its full value.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This ruling is the counterpart to the previous one. If the tear is so extensive that the cloth loses most of its utility and is effectively destroyed, the wrongdoer must pay the full value of the cloth. The commentator distinguishes between a defect (partial loss) and destruction (total loss). Here, the cloth is no longer serviceable, so it is treated as destroyed property, and the owner is entitled to the complete market value. This is consistent with the general Hanafi principle that if property is rendered useless, it is considered damaged in full.

Key Terms
منافع (manafi'): Benefits or utilities of a property; here, the functional uses of cloth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the tear is large enough to nullify most benefits.
B) When the tear is in a visible area.
C) When the owner demands full value.
D) When the cloth is expensive.

Key Takeaway
An extensive tear that destroys most benefits of cloth is treated as destruction, not defect, and the wrongdoer must pay the full value.

Change of Usurped Property: Ownership Transfer
Ruling with proof on ownership transfer upon transformation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership transfer when usurped property changes in name or primary benefits, and apply it to examples like grinding wheat or slaughtering an animal.

Core Idea
When usurped property loses its legal name or primary benefits, ownership transfers to the usurper—compensation must be paid before use.

This lesson explores a key Hanafi principle in the law of usurpation: if the usurper alters the property so fundamentally that its name or primary benefits disappear, ownership shifts automatically. However, the usurper cannot enjoy the transformed asset until he compensates the original owner. We will examine this rule through the example of wheat ground into flour and a prophetic hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled? When usurped property changes by the usurper's act until its name or major benefits are lost, the original owner's ownership ceases and the usurper becomes owner. But the usurper cannot benefit until compensation is paid.
Focus on the two conditions: loss of name or loss of primary benefits.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith of the woman who took a sheep without permission, slaughtered it, and served it to the Prophet. The Prophet did not eat and ordered it to be fed to prisoners.
This hadith is from Sunan Abu Dawud, transmitted by Aasim ibn Kulayb.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith shows that after slaughter (change from live sheep to meat), the woman became owner of the meat. However, since she had not compensated the original owner, the Prophet refrained from eating and ordered it given to prisoners, indicating that use is unlawful until compensation is paid.
The change of name (sheep to meat) triggers ownership transfer.
4
Apply the ruling to other transformations
Extend to any irreversible change: wheat to flour, grapes to wine, yarn to cloth, timber to furniture. In each case, if the name or primary benefit is lost, ownership transfers.
The transformation must be by the act of the usurper.
5
State the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position: the usurper owns the transformed property but must pay the value of the original property before using it. This is from the zahir al-riwayah.
The original owner's claim now becomes a debt claim for value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا تغيرت العين المغصوبة بفعل الغاصب حتى زال اسمها واعظم منافعها زال ملك المغصوب منه عنها وملكها الغاصب
اور حدیث اوپر گزرگئی (بخاری شریف، نمبر۲۴۸۲)
خرق : پھاڑا، پیرا : تھوڑا۔
اگر بدل جائے عین مغصوب غاصب کے فعل سے یہاں تک کہ اس کا نام زائل ہو جائے یا اعلی منافع ختم ہو جائے تو مغصوب منہ کی ملک اس سے زائل ہو جائے گی اور غاصب اس کا مالک ہو جائے گا۔ لیکن اس کے لئے اس چیز سے فائدہ اٹھانا جائز نہیں ہے یہاں تک کہ مغصوب کا بدلہ ادا کر دے۔ اس عبارت میں کئی باتیں بیان کی ہیں۔ ایک تو یہ کہ غاصب نے مغصوب چیز کے ساتھ ایسی حرکت کی جس سے مغصوب چیز موجود تو ہے لیکن اب اس کا نام بدل کر کچھ اور ہو گیا مثلا گیہوں تھا اس کو غصب کر کے پیس لیا اب اس کا نام آٹا ہو گیا۔ پہلا نام گیہوں باقی نہیں رہا۔ البتہ معنوی طور پر گیہوں موجود ہے۔ اسی طرح گیہوں کی منفعت بونا ختم ہو گئی اب آٹے کی منفعت روٹی پکانا ہوگئی۔ ایسی صورت میں مصنف فرماتے ہیں کہ چیز کا نام زائل ہوتے ہی مالک کی ملکیت اس سے ختم ہو جائے گی اور غاصب اس کا مالک ہو جائے گا۔ لیکن جب تک اس کا ضمان ادانہ کر دے غاصب کے لئے اس سے فائدہ اٹھانا حلال نہیں ہے۔ جب مغصوب چیز کا نام بدل گیا یا منافع ختم ہو گئے تو اب غاصب کے ضمان میں داخل ہو گئی۔ اس لئے اب غاصب اس کا مالک ہو جائے گا
(۲) ایک حدیث میں اس کا اشارہ ہے۔ آپ ایک صحابی کو دفن کر کے قبرستان سے تشریف لائے ۔ ایک عورت نے آپ کی دعوت کی اور بکری ایک پڑوسن کے پاس سے لائی۔ پڑوسن کا شوہر گھر میں نہیں تھا جس کی وجہ سے بغیر اس کی اجازت کے لائی اور ذبح کر کے حضور اور صحابہ کی دعوت کی تو آپ نے نوش نہیں فرمایا اور فرمایا کہ اس کو قیدیوں کو کھلا دو۔ اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ بکری کو ذبح کرنے کے بعد دعوت کرنے والی عورت مالک ہو گئی اس لئے بکری کو مالک کی طرف واپس نہیں کیا۔ لیکن چونکہ ابھی اس کا عوض واپس نہیں کیا تھا اس لئے اس کا استعمال کرنا حلال نہیں تھا۔ اس لئے آپ نے نہیں کھایا بلکہ قیدیوں کو کھلا دیا۔

حاشیہ : (الف) انصار کے راوی نے فرمایا کہ ہم حضور کے ساتھ جنازے میں نکلے۔ پھر آپ نے فرمایا ایسا لگتا ہے کہ بکری کا گوشت بغیر اس کے مالک کی اجازت سے لیا ہے۔ پس دعوت کرنے والی عورت کو بلایا۔ کہنے لگی اے اللہ کے رسول میں نے مقام بقیع کی طرف بکری خریدنے کے لئے بھیجا پس نہیں پایا۔ پس اپنے پڑوسی کو خبر بھیجی جس نے بکری خریدی تھی کہ مجھ کو قیمت کے بدلے بکری بھیج دے پس وہ نہیں ملے۔ پھر میں نے اس کی بیوی کو خر بھیجی تو انہوں نے بکری بھیج دی تو حضور نے فرمایا قیدیوں کو یہ گوشت کھلا دو۔
And when the usurped property changes by the act of the usurper until its name and most benefits are lost, the ownership of the one from whom it was usurped ceases and the usurper owns it. And the hadith has already passed (Bukhari, no. 2482). Tear: rent; small: little. If the usurped property changes by the usurper's act to the extent that its name is lost or its major benefits are lost, then the ownership of the original owner ceases and the usurper becomes its owner. But it is not permissible for him to benefit from it until he pays compensation for the usurped item. In this text, several points are stated: one is that the usurper did something to the usurped property so that the property exists but its name has changed to something else, e.g., wheat was usurped and ground, so its name becomes flour. The original name 'wheat' no longer remains, though materially wheat exists. Similarly, the benefit of sowing wheat is gone, now the benefit of flour is baking bread. In such a case, the author says that as soon as the name is lost, the owner's ownership ends and the usurper becomes its owner. But until he pays its compensation, it is not lawful for the usurper to benefit from it. When the name of the usurped property changes or benefits are lost, it enters the liability of the usurper, so the usurper becomes its owner. A hadith indicates this: The Prophet came from a funeral, a woman invited him and brought a sheep from a neighbor whose husband was not home, so she took it without permission and slaughtered it to serve the Prophet and companions. The Prophet did not eat and said, 'Feed it to the prisoners.' From this hadith, it is understood that after slaughtering the sheep, the hostess became its owner, so the sheep was not returned to the original owner. But since she had not yet paid its compensation, it was not lawful for her to use it, so the Prophet did not eat and fed it to prisoners. The hadith text: 'Aasim ibn Kulayb narrated from his father from a man of the Ansar...'
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage presents a key principle in Hanafi law of usurpation: when the usurper so alters the property that its name (legal category) or its primary benefits are lost, ownership transfers from the original owner to the usurper. The example given is wheat ground into flour: the wheat no longer exists as wheat in the legal sense, so the original owner cannot reclaim the exact item; instead, ownership shifts to the usurper, who must compensate the original owner with the value of the wheat. However, the usurper cannot enjoy the transformed property until he pays compensation. The hadith of the woman who took a sheep without permission, slaughtered it, and served it to the Prophet supports this: the Prophet did not eat because the sheep was not yet compensated, but he ordered it to be fed to prisoners, indicating that the woman had become owner after slaughter (since the name changed from live sheep to meat) but could not benefit until she paid. This hadith is from Abu Dawud. The commentator emphasizes that the usurper becomes liable for the value, and ownership transfers automatically upon the transformation.

Key Terms
عين مغصوبة (ayn maghsubah): The specific usurped property in its original form.
زوال الاسم (zawal al-ism): Loss of the legal name/category of the property (e.g., wheat to flour).
اعظم منافع (a'zam al-manafi'): The primary or most significant benefits of the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a usurper grinds usurped wheat into flour?
A) The original owner can reclaim the flour.
B) The usurper becomes owner of the flour but must pay the value of wheat before using it.
C) The usurper must return the flour as is without compensation.
D) The ownership remains with the original owner and the usurper is only liable for the grinding cost.

Key Takeaway
If usurped property changes in name or primary benefits, ownership transfers to the usurper, who must compensate the original owner before using the property.

6 min
Ownership Transfer of Usurped Property Upon Fundamental Change
Ruling when usurped property's name or primary benefit changes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership transfer when usurped property's name or primary benefit changes, identify the dalil from the hadith, and distinguish between the relied-upon position and the views of Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
The usurper becomes owner when the property's identity changes, but cannot benefit until compensation.

This passage from the chapter on usurpation (ghasb) addresses a key scenario: what happens when the usurper alters the property so fundamentally that its name and primary utility are lost? The Hanafi school rules that ownership transfers to the usurper, but with a crucial restriction—benefit is prohibited until the original owner is compensated. The author supports this with a Prophetic hadith and then notes a disagreement within the school and between madhhabs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for ownership transfer
The passage states that if the usurper changes the property such that its name (ism) and greatest benefits (a'zam manafi') are lost, the original owner's ownership ceases. Determine what constitutes 'name' and 'greatest benefit'.
Examples: goat becomes roasted meat; wheat becomes flour.
2
Determine the usurper's legal status
The usurper becomes owner (malik) but is liable for the value (daman) and cannot benefit from the property until he pays the replacement (badal). This is the core ruling.
Ownership transfer is automatic, but the right to use is suspended.
3
Examine the hadith proof
The hadith about the woman who took a neighbor's goat without permission, slaughtered it, and the Prophet ﷺ refused to eat it and ordered it given to prisoners. Analyze how this demonstrates that she became owner (by slaughtering) but could not benefit.
The Prophet's action indicates prohibition of use, not denial of ownership.
4
Compare opposing views
Abu Yusuf holds the usurper can benefit before compensation because he is owner. Al-Shafi'i holds ownership does not transfer until compensation. Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that of Abu Hanifa: no benefit before compensation.
5
Apply to given examples
Apply the rule to the four examples: goat slaughtered/cooked, wheat ground, iron made into sword, brass made into vessel. Confirm ownership transfer and prohibition of use.
The change must be permanent and affect both name and primary benefit.

عورت مالک ہو گئی اس لئے بکری کو مالک کی طرف واپس نہیں کیا۔ لیکن چونکہ ابھی اس کا عوض واپس نہیں کیا تھا اس لئے اس کا استعمال کرنا حلال نہیں تھا۔ اس لئے آپ نے نہیں کھایا بلکہ قیدیوں کو کھلا دیا۔ حدیث کا شاة فلم اجد فارسلت الى جار لي قد اشترى شاة ان ارسل الى بها بثمنها فلم يوجد فارسلت الى امرأته فارسلت إلى بها فقال رسول الله الله الله الطعمیه الاساری (الف) (ابوداؤد حاشیہ : (الف) انصار کے راوی نے فرمایا کہ ہم حضور کے ساتھ جنازے میں نکلے۔ پھر آپ نے فرمایا ایسا لگتا ہے کہ بکری کا گوشت بغیر اس کے مالک کی اجازت سے لیا ہے۔ پس دعوت کرنے والی عورت کو بلایا۔ کہنے لگی اے اللہ کے رسول میں نے مقام بقیع کی طرف بکری خریدنے کے لئے بھیجا پس نہیں پایا۔ پس اپنے پڑوسی کو خبر بھیجی جس نے بکری خریدی تھی کہ مجھ کو قیمت کے بدلے بکری بھیج دے پس وہ نہیں ملے۔ پھر میں نے اس کی بیوی کو خر بھیجی تو انہوں نے بکری بھیج دی تو حضور نے فرمایا قیدیوں کو یہ گوشت کھلا دو۔ فائده امام ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں کہ غاصب مالک ہو جائے گا اور ضمان دینے سے پہلے اس سے استفادہ بھی کر سکتا ہے۔ کیونکہ غاصب اس کا مالک ہو گیا۔ اور امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ منصوب کے ضمان دینے سے پہلے غاصب اس چیز کا مالک نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ مالک دینے پر راضی نہیں ہوا ہے۔ اور نہ ابھی اس کا بدلہ ادا کیا ہے۔ المغصوب منہ : اس سے مراد مالک ہے کیونکہ اسی سے چیز غصب کی ہے۔ (۱۵۶۳] (۱۳) یہ جیسے کہ بکری غصب کر کے اس کو ذبح کر لیا اور اس کو بھون لیا یا اس کو پکالیایا گیہوں غصب کیا اور اس کو پیس لیایا لوہا غصب کیا اور اس کو تلوار بنالیا یا پیتل غصب کیا اور اس کو برتن بنالیا۔ اس عبارت میں شی منصوب کے نام بدل جانے اور اس کے اعظم منافع کے ختم ہو جانے کی چار مثالیں دی ہیں۔ اور اوپر کے اصول پر متفرع کی ہیں۔ مثلا بکری غصب کی اور اس کو ذبح کر کے بھون لیا یا پکا لیا تو اس کا نام اب بکری نہیں رہا بلکہ سالن اور گوشت ہو گیا تو چونکہ نام بدل گیا اس لئے مالک کی ملکیت زائل ہو گئی اور غاصب اس کا مالک بن گیا۔ یا گیہوں غصب کیا اور پیس کر آٹا بنا دیا یا لوہا نصب کر کے تلوار بنا لیا یا پیتل غصب کر کے اس کو برتن بنالیا توان کے نام زائل ہو گئے اور اعظم منافع زائل ہو گئے اور اب دوسرے منافع ہو گئے اس لئے غاصب مالک ہو گیا۔ البتہ اس کا بدلہ دیئے بغیر اس کو استعمال کرنا اچھا نہیں ہے۔ اوپر گزرگئی ہے۔ شواها : بھون لیا، طحن : پیں دیا، صفر : پیتل، آئیه : برتن۔
When the usurped property is changed by the act of the usurper such that its name and greatest benefits are lost, the ownership of the one from whom it was usurped ceases and the usurper becomes its owner, and he is liable for its compensation, and it is not permissible for him to benefit from it until he pays its replacement. This is like one who usurps a goat and slaughters it and roasts it or cooks it, or usurps wheat and grinds it, or iron and makes it into a sword, or brass and makes it into a vessel. ... (Then the hadith and Urdu commentary follow, including the benefit that Abu Yusuf says the usurper can benefit before compensation while al-Shafi'i says he does not become owner until compensation; and the explanation of examples.)
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter of Ghasb (usurpation). It states that if the usurper (ghasib) physically alters the usurped property (maghsub) to such an extent that its original name is lost (e.g., wheat becomes flour) or its primary utility is destroyed (e.g., grain can no longer be sown, but flour can be baked), then the original owner's ownership is transferred to the usurper. However, the usurper is not allowed to benefit from the property until he pays its equivalent value (badal). The author provides a hadith about a woman who took a neighbor's goat without permission, slaughtered and cooked it, and the Prophet ﷺ did not eat it but ordered it to be given to prisoners, indicating that the woman became owner but could not use it until compensation. Then a fa'idah (benefit) is mentioned: Imam Abu Yusuf holds that the usurper can benefit even before compensation because he is now the owner, while Imam al-Shafi'i holds that ownership does not transfer until compensation because the original owner did not consent. The relied-upon Hanafi position is that it is not permissible to benefit before compensation. The passage then gives examples of usurping a goat, wheat, iron, and brass, and the ruling that the usurper becomes owner but must not use the item until he pays the replacement.

Key Terms
الغصب (al-ghasb): Usurpation; taking another's property by force or without right.
العين المغصوبة (al-ayn al-maghsubah): The specific property that was usurped.
الضمان (ad-daman): Liability to compensate; here it means the obligation to pay the value or replacement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, when a usurper changes the usurped property so that its name is lost, what is the ruling?
A) The original owner retains ownership and the usurper must return the item as is.
B) The usurper becomes owner but cannot benefit until he compensates the original owner.
C) The usurper becomes owner and may immediately benefit from it.
D) The property is considered destroyed and the usurper must pay its value, but ownership remains with the original owner.

Key Takeaway
When usurped property changes in name and primary benefit, the usurper becomes the owner but is prohibited from using it until he compensates the original owner.

Gold and Silver Exception: No Ownership Transfer Upon Coining
Exception to ownership transfer in ghasp for gold and silver

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership of usurped gold and silver when transformed into coins or vessels, and explain why their essential nature as thaman prevents transfer of ownership.

Core Idea
Gold and silver retain their legal identity as thaman (price) even when coined or shaped; thus, ownership does not transfer to the usurper.

You have learned that usurping a grain and grinding it transfers ownership to the usurper. But gold and silver are different. In this lesson, we examine an exception: when gold or silver is usurped and then coined into dirhams or fashioned into vessels, the original owner's ownership does not end. This ruling is based on the fundamental nature of gold and silver as thaman (price).

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule in ghasp
Recall the general rule: if a usurper takes someone's property and changes its form (e.g., grinding wheat into flour), ownership transfers to the usurper and the original owner is entitled to compensation.
This rule applies to most goods that are considered 'consumables' (mustahlikat).
2
State the exception for gold and silver
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, gold and silver are exceptions. Even if the usurper coins them into dirhams or makes them into vessels, ownership does not transfer to the usurper.
The original owner retains ownership of the metal in its new form.
3
Explain the legal cause (illah)
The illah is that gold and silver are thaman (price). Their primary benefit is being a measure of value and wealth, which remains unchanged regardless of physical form. Therefore, they are not considered 'consumed' by the transformation.
Thaman is a legal category: items that serve as a medium of exchange are not subject to ownership transfer via istihlak (consumption).
4
Note the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
The text attributes this ruling to Imam Abu Hanifa. It is implied that the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) may hold that ownership does transfer, as with other goods. However, the mufta bihi position follows Abu Hanifa.
This ikhtilaf is based on whether the thaman status overrides the general rule of ownership by transformation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان غصب فضة او ذهبا فضربها دراهم او دنانيرا وآنية لم يزل ملك المغصوب منه عنها (متن) اور اگر چاندی یا سونا غصب کیا اوران کو درہم یا دینارڈھال لیایا برتن بنالیا توان سے مالک کی ملکیت ختم نہیں ہوگی امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک۔ ا اصل قاعدہ یہ ہے کہ سونا اور چاندی چاہے ڈلی کی حالت میں ہوں چاہے سکوں کی حالت میں ہوں اور چاہے برتن کی حالت میں ہوں پھر بھی وہ سونا اور چاندی ہی ہیں اور ان کوشن ہی گنے جائیں گے ۔ اس لئے سونا چاندی غصب کر کے غاصب ان کو کسی حال میں بھی ڈھال لے ان کا اصل نام شمن باقی ہے اور اعظم منافع یعنی جمعیت باقی ہے اس لئے مالک کی ملکیت ختم نہیں ہوگی۔ اور غاصب ان کا مالک نہیں ہو گا ۔ یہ امام
And if he usurps silver or gold and strikes them as dirhams or dinars or vessels, the ownership of the one from whom it was usurped does not cease. And if silver or gold is usurped and they are coined into dirhams or dinars or made into vessels, then the original owner's ownership does not end, according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The basic principle is that gold and silver, whether in the form of ingots, coins, or vessels, are still gold and silver, and they are considered as such. Therefore, if the usurper molds them in any way, their original name (thaman) remains and their greatest benefit (wealth) remains, so the original owner's ownership does not end, and the usurper does not become the owner. This is according to Imam Abu Hanifa.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage states an exception to the previous ruling: gold and silver (as precious metals) retain their essential nature regardless of physical transformation. Coining ingots into dirhams or fashioning vessels does not change their fundamental identity as thaman (price/wealth). Therefore, the original owner's ownership does not transfer to the usurper, unlike the case of wheat ground into flour. The text attributes this ruling to Imam Abu Hanifa, implying that there may be a difference of opinion (likely from the Sahibayn, but not stated here). The key principle is that gold and silver are always considered thaman (currency) and their primary benefit (being wealth) remains intact, so no transfer occurs.

Key Terms
الثمن (ath-thaman): Price, value; here it refers to the intrinsic nature of gold and silver as media of exchange.
الفضة والذهب (al-fiddah wa adh-dhahab): Silver and gold as raw metals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does ownership of gold and silver not transfer to the usurper when it is coined into dirhams or made into vessels?
A) Because the usurper did not intend to own it.
B) Because gold and silver are considered thaman (price) and their primary benefit (wealth) remains unchanged.
C) Because the original owner explicitly forbade such use.
D) Because the hadith mentioned earlier applies only to food items.

Key Takeaway
Gold and silver, as thaman, retain their legal identity regardless of physical transformation; thus, usurping and transforming them does not transfer ownership to the usurper.
