Usurper's Ownership upon Change of Name and Benefit
Ruling on usurper owning property after radical transformation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a usurper becomes owner of the usurped property and explain the Hanafi position on using the property before compensation.

Core Idea
A usurper owns property after its name and primary benefit change, but must pay compensation before using it.

This passage deals with a key fiqh issue: when does a usurper become owner of property that has been radically transformed? The Hanafi school holds that destruction or transformation of the property's name and primary benefit transfers ownership to the usurper, but use before compensation is disliked. We will examine the ruling, its proof from a prophetic incident, and the disagreements within the Hanafi school and with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Conditions
State the ruling: when a usurped property is destroyed or transformed such that its name and primary benefit change, the usurper becomes owner. List the conditions: (1) destruction/transformation, (2) change of name, (3) loss of primary benefit.
The four examples (sheep, wheat, iron, brass) illustrate these conditions.
2
Analyse the Proof (Dalil)
Examine the hadith of the goat offered to the Prophet. The Prophet did not return the goat nor drank its milk, indicating the goat became property of the woman. His refraining shows use without compensation is not commendable.
The proof is a hadith ahad (zanni al-thubut), which is sufficient for a ruling of makruh tanzihi.
3
Understand the Intra-Hanafi Disagreement
Note that Imam Abu Yusuf holds the usurper may benefit before compensation, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold he cannot. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that he should not benefit until compensation is paid.
This disagreement is based on whether ownership alone suffices for lawful use.
4
Understand the Cross-Madhhab Disagreement
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that ownership does not transfer until compensation is paid, because the owner did not consent and no substitute has been given. The Hanafi response is that the transformation severs the original ownership.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is when the usurper becomes owner and whether he may use the property.
5
Apply the Ruling to Similar Cases
Apply the conditions to new scenarios: e.g., stolen timber made into furniture, or stolen grapes made into wine. Determine if name and primary benefit have changed.
If the change is reversible (e.g., painting a car), ownership does not transfer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وضمنها ولا يحل له الانتفاع بها حتى يؤدى بدلها [۱۵۶۳] (۱۳) وهذا كمن غصب شاة فذبحها وشواها او طبخها او غصب حنطة فطحنها او حديدا فاتخذه سيفا او صفرا فعمله آنية اس حدیث میں بکری دعوت کرنے والی عورت کی ملکیت ہوگئی اسی لئے اس کو واپس نہیں کیا۔ لیکن استعمال کرنا اچھا نہیں تھا اس لئے آپ نے نوش نہیں فرمایا اصول شی مغصوب کی ہلاکت سے غاصب مالک ہو جائے گا چاہے ابھی بدلہ نہ دیا ہو۔ فائده امام ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں کہ غاصب مالک ہو جائے گا اور ضمان دینے سے پہلے اس سے استفادہ بھی کر سکتا ہے۔ کیونکہ غاصب اس کا مالک ہو گیا۔ اور امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ منصوب کے ضمان دینے سے پہلے غاصب اس چیز کا مالک نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ مالک دینے پر راضی نہیں ہوا ہے۔ اور نہ ابھی اس کا بدلہ ادا کیا ہے۔ المغصوب منہ : اس سے مراد مالک ہے کیونکہ اسی سے چیز غصب کی ہے۔ (۱۵۶۳] (۱۳) یہ جیسے کہ بکری غصب کر کے اس کو ذبح کر لیا اور اس کو بھون لیا یا اس کو پکالیایا گیہوں غصب کیا اور اس کو پیس لیایا لوہا غصب کیا اور اس کو تلوار بنالیا یا پیتل غصب کیا اور اس کو برتن بنالیا۔ اس عبارت میں شی منصوب کے نام بدل جانے اور اس کے اعظم منافع کے ختم ہو جانے کی چار مثالیں دی ہیں۔ اور اوپر کے اصول پر متفرع کی ہیں۔ مثلا بکری غصب کی اور اس کو ذبح کر کے بھون لیا یا پکا لیا تو اس کا نام اب بکری نہیں رہا بلکہ سالن اور گوشت ہو گیا تو چونکہ نام بدل گیا اس لئے مالک کی ملکیت زائل ہو گئی اور غاصب اس کا مالک بن گیا۔ یا گیہوں غصب کیا اور پیس کر آٹا بنا دیا یا لوہا نصب کر کے تلوار بنا لیا یا پیتل غصب کر کے اس کو برتن بنالیا توان کے نام زائل ہو گئے اور اعظم منافع زائل ہو گئے اور اب دوسرے منافع ہو گئے اس لئے غاصب مالک ہو گیا۔ البتہ اس کا بدلہ دیئے بغیر اس کو استعمال کرنا اچھا نہیں ہے۔ شواها : بھون لیا، طحن : پیں دیا، صفر : پیتل، آئیه : برتن۔
And it is guaranteed and it is not permissible for him to benefit from it until he pays its substitute. [1563] (13) And this is like one who usurped a sheep, slaughtered it, roasted it or cooked it; or usurped wheat and ground it; or iron and made it a sword; or brass and made it a vessel. In this hadith, the goat became the property of the woman who invited, so it was not returned. But using it was not good, so the Prophet did not drink. Principle: The usurped thing perishing makes the usurper owner, even if he has not yet paid the substitute. Benefit: Imam Abu Yusuf says: The usurper becomes owner and may benefit from it before paying compensation, because the usurper has become its owner. Imam al-Shafi'i says: Before paying compensation, the usurper does not become owner, because the owner did not consent to give it and no substitute has been paid. Al-maghsub minhu: This means the owner, because from him the thing was usurped. (1563] (13) This is like: usurping a goat and slaughtering it and roasting or cooking it; usurping wheat and grinding it; usurping iron and making a sword; usurping brass and making a vessel. This phrase gives four examples of the change of name and loss of the primary benefit. And they are branched from the above principle. For example, a goat was usurped and slaughtered and roasted or cooked, its name is no longer goat but curry and meat. So since the name changed, the owner's ownership is removed and the usurper becomes owner. Or wheat usurped and ground into flour; or iron and made a sword; or brass and made a vessel; their names are removed and the primary benefits are removed, and other benefits come, so the usurper becomes owner. However, using it without paying compensation is not good. Shawaha: roasted, Tahan: ground, Sufr: brass, Aniyah: vessel.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on usurpation where the usurped property undergoes a radical transformation. The Arabic matn establishes that when a usurped item is destroyed or changed in identity, the usurper becomes its owner but must pay compensation. The commentary then provides four examples: sheep (slaughtered and cooked), wheat (ground), iron (made into a sword), and brass (made into a vessel). In each case, the item's name and primary benefit change, so ownership transfers to the usurper. The passage also includes a hadith about a goat offered to the Prophet, illustrating that the goat became property of the woman who invited him, but the Prophet did not drink because use without compensation is disliked. The principle is that destruction of the usurped property makes the usurper owner. A benefit mentions disagreement: Imam Abu Yusuf holds that the usurper becomes owner immediately and may benefit before paying, while Imam al-Shafi'i holds that ownership does not transfer until compensation is paid. The commentary emphasizes that the examples are applications of the rule that change of name and primary benefit removes the owner's ownership. Note: The Hanafi position is that the usurper becomes owner upon destruction/change, but use before compensation is makruh tanzihi (disliked). The authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah as the base ruling is from the core texts.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation: taking another's property wrongfully and forcefully.
بدل (badal): Substitute: the compensation (qimah or mithl) paid for the usurped property.
اعظم منافع (a'zam al-manafi'): Primary benefit: the main customary use of the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does a usurper become the owner of a usurped sheep that he slaughtered and cooked?
A) Only after paying the compensation
B) Immediately upon slaughtering, but cannot use until compensation paid
C) Never, because the owner retains ownership
D) Only if the owner agrees

Key Takeaway
The usurper owns the property upon transformation but must pay compensation before using it.

Why Melting Gold Does Not Transfer Ownership: The Hanafi View
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ownership of melted gold/silver

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on usurping gold/silver and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Gold and silver are permanent thaman; melting them does not end the owner's ownership.

When someone usurps gold or silver and melts it into coins or vessels, does ownership transfer? Imam Abu Hanifa says no, because gold and silver are timeless currency; the Sahibayn argue otherwise. Let's unpack this disagreement.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling of Abu Hanifa
Identify that Imam Abu Hanifa holds the owner retains ownership regardless of the form (ingot, coin, vessel).
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the basis: thaman remains
Understand why Abu Hanifa says ownership stays: gold and silver are intrinsically thaman (price). Their name and primary benefit (value) do not change with form.
Thaman is the essence that makes them currency.
3
Contrast with the Sahibayn's view
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that if the form changes so does the name (e.g., bullion to coin), ownership transfers, like other materials.
They apply qiyas to other goods.
4
Conclude with the tarjih
Later Hanafi scholars prefer Abu Hanifa's view, making it the operative fatwa.
Authority: Al-Hidayah, Ibn Abidin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۱۵۶۴) (۱۴) اور اگر چاندی یا سونا غصب کیا اوران کو درہم یا دینارڈھال لیایا برتن بنالیا توان سے مالک کی ملکیت ختم نہیں ہوگی امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک۔ ا اصل قاعدہ یہ ہے کہ سونا اور چاندی چاہے ڈلی کی حالت میں ہوں چاہے سکوں کی حالت میں ہوں اور چاہے برتن کی حالت میں ہوں پھر بھی وہ سونا اور چاندی ہی ہیں اور ان کوشن ہی گنے جائیں گے ۔ اس لئے سونا چاندی غصب کر کے غاصب ان کو کسی حال میں بھی ڈھال لے ان کا اصل نام شمن باقی ہے اور اعظم منافع یعنی جمعیت باقی ہے اس لئے مالک کی ملکیت ختم نہیں ہوگی۔ اور غاصب ان کا مالک نہیں ہو گا ۔ یہ امام ابو حنیفہ کی رائے ہے۔ صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ سونے اور چاندی میں بھی ایسی تبدیلی کر دے کہ ان کا نام بدل جائے تو اس سے مالک کی ملکیت زائل ہو جائے گی اور غاصب کی ملکیت ہو جائے گی۔ اس لئے کہ اب نہ اس کا وہ نام باقی رہا اور نہ اس کی وہ منفعت باقی رہی۔ نوال سونے اور چاندی کے بھی نام بدل جائے تو مالک کی ملکیت ختم ہو جائے گی اور غاصب کی ملکیت ہو جائے گی۔
1564) (14) And if one usurps silver or gold and melts them into dirhams or dinars or makes vessels, then the owner's ownership does not cease according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The basic principle is that silver and gold, whether in the form of ingots, coins, or vessels, are still silver and gold and will be counted as such. Therefore, if a usurper usurps silver or gold and melts them into any form, their original name (thaman) remains and the primary benefit (wealth) remains, so the owner's ownership does not end. And the usurper will not become owner. This is the view of Imam Abu Hanifa. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: In silver and gold also, if one makes such a change that their name changes, then the owner's ownership is removed and the usurper becomes owner. Because now neither the name nor the benefit remains. Yes, if the name of silver and gold changes, ownership ends and usurper becomes owner.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage addresses the specific case of usurping silver or gold and melting them into different forms (coins or vessels). Imam Abu Hanifa holds that ownership does not transfer because silver and gold retain their essential nature and name (thaman) regardless of form, and the primary benefit (being a store of value) remains intact. Therefore, the usurper does not become owner. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree: they argue that if the form changes so that the name changes (e.g., bullion becomes coin or vessel), then ownership transfers, as with other materials. The Hanafi school relies on Abu Hanifa's view in this matter, as the authority of silver/gold as thaman is considered immutable. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute (Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn). The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa, as per later Hanafi authorities like Al-Hidayah. The authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah since the ruling appears in the six books.

Key Terms
ثمن (thaman): Price or medium of exchange: silver and gold are intrinsically valuable as currency.
صاحبین (sahibayn): The two companions: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, disciples of Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what happens if someone usurps gold and melts it into coins?
A) The usurper becomes owner and must pay compensation
B) The original owner remains owner; the coins must be returned
C) The gold becomes ownerless
D) The gold is considered destroyed and qimah is due

Key Takeaway
When usurping gold or silver, the original owner retains ownership even if the form changes, according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Qiyas and the Uniqueness of Thaman: Analysing the Proof
Qiyas analysis in the gold/silver melting dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse the qiyas structure in the dispute over melted gold and identify why the illa (effective cause) is contested.

Core Idea
Qiyas fails where the illa is absent: gold's thaman status overrides change of form.

The Sahibayn applied qiyas from other materials to gold and silver, but Abu Hanifa rejected it because the illa of 'change of name and benefit' does not apply when the essence (thaman) remains. Let's dissect this qiyas step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the asl (original case)
The asl is other materials (e.g., sheep, wheat, iron) where change of form transfers ownership to the usurper.
This is the established ruling from general ghasb law.
2
Identify the far (new case)
The far is silver and gold melted into a different form (ingot to coin or vessel).
The Sahibayn argue that the same ruling should apply.
3
Extract the illa (effective cause)
The illa is 'change of name and primary benefit'. Both Sahibayn and Abu Hanifa agree on this illa in principle.
The dispute is whether this illa is present in gold/silver.
4
Analyse the presence of illa in gold/silver
Abu Hanifa says the name 'thaman' and the benefit of being a store of value remain, so the illa is absent. Sahibayn say the specific form changes the name (e.g., 'ingot' to 'coin'), so the illa is present.
This is the crux of the ikhtilaf.
5
Conclude the hukm
Since the illa is not fulfilled according to Abu Hanifa, the qiyas fails, and ownership does not transfer. This is the mufta bihi.
The ruling is based on the unique nature of thaman.

Usurped Beam Built into Wall: Ownership Transfer by Istihsan
Ruling on usurping a beam and building upon it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a usurped beam integrated into a structure and explain the legal basis in istihsan and the principle of preventing loss.

Core Idea
A usurper becomes owner of a beam built into a wall and pays its price to avoid excessive loss.

This lesson examines a specific case of ghasb (usurpation) where the usurped item becomes structurally integral to the usurper's property. The Hanafi school, prioritizing harm prevention (la darar), transfers ownership to the usurper with compensation, diverging from the Shafi'i position. We will explore the legal reasoning (istidlal) behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
The usurper becomes owner of the beam and must pay its market price (qimah) to the original owner. Ownership is transferred despite the beam not being destroyed or altered.
This is the mufta bihi position from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Understand the rationale: istihsan based on harm prevention
Although the beam is intact, it is now attached to the usurper's property. Forcible removal would cause disproportionate loss to the usurper (e.g., wall collapse). Hanafi law applies istihsan (juristic preference) to avoid this harm, overriding the default rule that the owner can reclaim the property.
The dalil is qiyas jali (clear analogy) but istihsan overrides it due to the specific circumstance.
3
Compare with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the owner retains ownership because the beam is neither destroyed nor changed in name or essence. The owner can demolish the wall to retrieve it.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf (valid difference of opinion).
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Hanafi law, the usurper keeps the beam and pays compensation; the wall remains standing. Under Shafi'i law, the owner can force demolition and take the beam.
The Hanafi ruling is more harm-preventive.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih (preference)
Later Hanafi authorities (e.g., Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah) adopt the view that ownership transfers, citing the maxim 'la darar wa la dirar' (no harm and no reciprocation of harm).
The authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah; the ruling is relied upon for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۵۶۵] (۱۵) کسی نے شہتیر غصب کیا اور اس پر عمارت بنالی تو مالک کی ملکیت اس سے زائل ہو جائے گی اور غاصب کو اس کی قیمت لازم ہوگی۔ یہ مسئلہ اس قاعدے پر ہے کہ مغصوب چیز نہ ہلاک ہوئی ہے اور نہ اس میں کوئی تبدیلی ہوئی ہے البتہ وہ غاصب کے مال کے ساتھ چپک گئی ہے کہ اب اگر منصوب چیز کو غاصب کے مال سے الگ کرتے ہیں تو غاصب کا بہت بڑا نقصان ہو جاتا ہے۔ اس لئے اس صورت میں بھی مالک کی ملکیت زائل ہو جائے گی اور غاصب کی ملکیت ہو جائے گی۔ مالک کو اس کی چیز واپس دیتے ہیں تو غاصب کی دیوار گر جائے گی اور اس کا نقصان ہوگا اور مالک کو اس کی قیمت دلوائیں تو اس کا نقصان نہیں ہے بلکہ شہتیر کی مکافات ہو جائے گی اس لئے شہتیر کی قیمت دلوانا بہتر ہے۔ ا ممکن ہو تو غاصب کو بھی نقصان سے بچانے کی کوشش کی جائے گی۔ امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ شہتیر مالک کی ہے اور وہ ہلاک بھی نہیں ہوئی ہے اور نہ اس کا نام بدلا ہے اس لئے مالک کی ملکیت زائل نہیں ہوگی اس لئے وہ واپس لینا چاہے تو غاصب کی دیوار توڑ وا کر لے سکتا ہے۔ ساجہ : شہتیر ، درمیان کی وہ موٹی اور بھی لکڑی جس پر چھپر کھڑی کرتے ہیں۔
[1565] (15) Someone usurped a beam and built upon it. Then the owner's ownership is removed from it and the usurper is liable for its price. This ruling is based on the principle that the usurped thing is neither destroyed nor changed, but it has become attached to the usurper's property such that if the usurped item is separated from the usurper's property, great loss would occur to the usurper. Therefore, in this case too, the owner's ownership is removed and the usurper becomes owner. If we give the item back to the owner, the usurper's wall would collapse and he would suffer loss; but if we give its price to the owner, he suffers no loss and the beam is compensated. So giving the beam's price is better. If possible, we try to avoid loss to the usurper. Imam al-Shafi'i says: The beam belongs to the owner, it is not destroyed nor has its name changed, so the owner's ownership does not cease; if he wants to take it back, he can demolish the usurper's wall and take it. Sajjah: beam, the thick middle wood on which a roof is supported.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage deals with a usurped beam used in construction. The Hanafi ruling is that the usurper becomes owner and must pay the beam's price (qimah). The rationale is that although the beam itself is neither destroyed nor changed in essence, it has become structurally integral to the usurper's building. Forcing its removal would cause excessive loss (darar) to the usurper. Hanafi law avoids such harm, preferring to compensate the owner with the value. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees: since the beam exists unchanged, the owner retains ownership and can demand its return even if it means demolishing the wall. The Hanafi position is preferred due to the principle of preventing loss (la darar wa la dirar). This is an inter-madhhab dispute. The authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah as the basis is in the core texts. The relied-upon Hanafi position is that ownership transfers to the usurper with payment of qimah.

Key Terms
ساجة (sajjah): Beam: a thick wooden log used as a main support in roofing.
قیمت (qimah): Market value compensation for the usurped item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if a usurped beam is built into a wall, what is the ruling?
A) The beam must be returned even if it demolishes the wall.
B) The usurper becomes owner and pays its market price.
C) The beam is considered destroyed and mithl is due.
D) The owner can claim double its price.

Key Takeaway
When a usurped item becomes structurally integral to another's property, Hanafi law transfers ownership to prevent loss, compensating the owner with the item's value.

Land Usurped with Trees or Buildings
Ruling on usurped land planted or built upon

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on land usurpation with trees or buildings and identify when compensation is due.

Core Idea
A usurper must remove his additions and return the land; if removal damages the land, compensation is wajib.

When land is usurped and the usurper plants trees or builds, the land must be returned empty. The Hanafi school emphasizes that the usurper has no right to keep his property on the owner's land. However, if removing the additions causes damage to the land, the owner can claim compensation. This lesson explores the exact obligations and the conditions for compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the primary ruling
Recognize that the act is ghasb (usurpation) with added trees or buildings. The primary ruling is that the usurper must remove his additions and return the land to its owner.
The land is not the usurper's, so he has no ownership claim.
2
Determine the compensation condition
Compensation is not automatic. It is only due if the removal process causes a defect (naqs) in the land, such as holes or soil erosion.
The owner may demand compensation for the diminished value of the land.
3
Understand the legal basis
The ruling derives from the principle that ownership is not transferred by trespass. The land must be restored to its original state, and any loss incurred by the owner must be remedied.
This is consistent with the maxim 'al-kharaj bi al-daman' but reversed: the usurper bears the risk.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۵۶۲) (۱۶) کسی نے زمین غصب کی اور اس میں پودا بود یا یا عمارت بنادی تو اس سے کہا جائے گا کہ پودے کو اکھاڑ دے اور عمارت توڑ دو اور خالی کر کے زمین کو مالک کی طرف واپس کر دو۔ پس اگر زمین میں نقص ہوتا ہو ان کے اکھاڑنے سے تو مالک کے لئے جائز ہے کہ اس کو اکھڑی ہوئی عمارت اور پودوں کی قیمت کا ضامن بنائے۔ ا کسی نے کسی کی زمین غصب کر لی اور اس میں پودا بود یا یا عمارت بنالی تو چونکہ زمین غاصب کی نہیں ہے اس لئے غاصب سے کہا جائے
[1562) (16) Someone usurped land and planted a tree or built a building. Then he will be told: Uproot the tree and demolish the building and return the land empty to its owner. Then if there is a defect in the land from the uprooting, it is permissible for the owner to make the usurper liable for the price of the uprooted building and trees. Someone usurped another's land and planted a tree or built a building, so since the land is not the usurper's, he will be told...
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on usurped land where the usurper has added trees or buildings. The default ruling is that the usurper must remove his additions and return the land empty to the owner. However, if the removal causes damage to the land (e.g., holes from uprooting trees), the owner may demand compensation for that damage from the usurper. The text is cut off, but it clarifies that since the land belongs to the original owner, the usurper has no right to keep his property on it; removal is obligatory. The compensation is for the diminished value of the land. This is consistent with the principle that land cannot be owned by trespassers. The authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah. No dispute mentioned here. Note: The numbering [۱۵۶۲] seems inconsistent; perhaps it is [۱۵۶۶] in the original.

ضمان (daman): Liability: the obligation to compensate for damage or loss.

Ruling on Usurping a Beam and Building on It: Ownership Transfer vs Original Owner's Right
Ruling on ownership transfer of usurped beam attached to building – Hanafi vs Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the transfer of ownership of a usurped beam attached to a building and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument, identifying the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
A usurped beam built into a wall transfers ownership to avoid great harm; the usurper pays its value.

This passage presents a dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding a beam (sājah) that has been usurped and then built into a building. At stake is whether the original owner retains ownership or whether it transfers to the usurper. The Hanafi school, using istihsān (juristic preference), rules that ownership transfers to prevent disproportionate harm, while Imam al-Shafi'i insists that since the beam is neither destroyed nor changed, ownership remains. We will analyze the reasoning and the practical difference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the usurper's ownership is established; the original owner loses ownership; the usurper must pay the value.
This is the position of Abu Hanifa and the relied-upon view in the school.
2
Identify the Hanafi dalil
The Hanafi proof is istihsān (juristic preference) based on preventing great harm to the usurper if the beam were removed. The attachment makes separation harmful, so ownership transfers to avoid greater harm.
This is a form of qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) attached to cases of transformation (istihālah).
3
State the Shafi'i position and its dalil
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that ownership remains with the original owner because the beam is neither destroyed nor changed in essence. Thus, the owner can reclaim it even if it means demolishing the wall.
The Shafi'i dalil is that original ownership persists as long as the item is recognizable.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i argument
Hanafi responds that although the beam is not destroyed, the attachment to the building causes such great harm (tasrīr) that it outweighs the original owner's right to the specific item. Hence, ownership transfers by necessity, analogous to cases where the item's form changes.
The principle of preventing harm (lā ḍarar wa lā ḍirār) underlies this istihsān.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Hanafi, the original owner receives compensation; under Shafi'i, he can demolish the wall to retrieve his beam. This difference affects property disputes and construction contracts.
The Hanafi position is muftā bihi (operative for fatwa) due to its practical expedience.

(15) Whoever usurps a beam and builds on it, the ownership of its owner is removed from it, and the usurper is liable for its value. Abu Hanifa's opinion. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say that if in gold and silver he makes such a change that their name changes, then the owner's ownership is removed and the usurper's ownership is established. Because now neither its original name remains nor its original benefit remains. Explanation: If gold and silver also change name, the owner's ownership ends and the usurper's ownership is established. (15) Whoever usurps a beam and builds on it, the owner's ownership is removed from it, and the usurper is obliged to pay its value. This ruling is based on the principle that the usurped item is neither destroyed nor changed, but it is attached to the usurper's property such that if the usurped item is separated from the usurper's property, great loss occurs to the usurper. Therefore, in this case too, the owner's ownership is removed and the usurper's ownership is established. If we return the item to the owner, the usurper's wall will collapse and he will suffer loss; if we make him pay its value to the owner, there is no loss to the owner, rather the beam is compensated. Hence, paying the value of the beam is better. If possible, effort is made to protect the usurper from loss as well. Imam al-Shafi'i says: The beam belongs to the owner and it is neither destroyed nor changed, so the owner's ownership is not removed; therefore, if he wants to take it back, he can demolish the usurper's wall and take it.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding a usurped beam built into a wall. The Hanafi position, held by Abu Hanifa, is that if a beam is built into a wall, the owner's ownership is removed and the usurper must pay its value. This is based on the principle of preventing great harm to the usurper (istihsan). The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) apply the same principle to gold and silver changed beyond recognition. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that since the beam is not destroyed or fundamentally changed, ownership remains with the original owner, who can reclaim it even if it means demolishing the wall. The text explains the Hanafi rationale: the beam is attached to the usurper's building; removing it would cause significant loss, so it is better to compensate the owner with the value and let the usurper keep the beam.

Key Terms
ساجة (sājah): A thick wooden beam used for roofing; the specific item usurped.
غصب (ghaṣb): Usurpation, taking property wrongfully without consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens when a usurper builds a beam (sājah) into his wall?
A) The original owner can demolish the wall to retrieve the beam.
B) The original owner's ownership of the beam is removed and the usurper must pay its value.
C) The usurper must return the same beam without any compensation.
D) The beam becomes jointly owned by both parties.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a usurped beam built into a wall transfers ownership to the usurper to avoid great harm; compensation is paid to the original owner.

Ruling on Usurping Land and Planting or Building
Ruling and hadith proofs on usurper's removal obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a usurper who plants or builds on another's land, cite the prophetic evidence, and explain the owner's option for compensation.

Core Idea
A usurper must remove his plants and buildings at his own cost — his wrongful act grants him no ownership.

This passage addresses a common scenario: someone wrongfully occupies land and improves it by planting or building. The Hanafi school provides a clear remedy: the usurper must undo his actions and restore the land to its original state. We will examine the ruling itself and the three hadiths that establish it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is usurping land (ghasb) and then planting trees or constructing a building on it. The ruling is that the usurper must remove (qal') everything he added and return the land empty to its owner.
The removal is obligatory (wajib) even if it causes loss to the usurper.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites three ahadith: (1) 'No right for a wrongful root' (Sa'id ibn Zayd), (2) the planter gets only his expenses (Rafi' ibn Khudayj), (3) the Prophet ordered removal of palm trees from another's land (Yahya ibn 'Urwa).
All three are ahad hadith, so their thubut is zanni, but they are consistently applied in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah for each hadith
First hadith: 'No right for a wrongful root' means the planter has no claim to the land or plants, so he must remove them. Second hadith: the planter gets only his expenses (nafaqah), not the crop — implying he must clear the land. Third hadith: the Prophet explicitly ordered removal, showing it is mandatory even for large trees.
The third hadith provides direct precedent (amal) for the ruling.
4
Note the owner's option for compensation
If removal damages the land (e.g., holes from uprooting), the owner may hold the usurper liable for the value of the removed items in their diminished state. This is not compensation for the plants themselves but for the loss caused by removal.
The usurper bears all costs; the owner is not forced to accept anything less than the land's original condition.
5
Consider cross-madhhab differences
The text does not discuss ikhtilaf, but it is known that Shafi'is sometimes allow the usurper to pay the land's value and keep the improvements if removal would be excessively harmful. The Hanafi position, based on these hadiths, insists on removal to uphold the owner's absolute right.
This difference is a thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) that affects real estate disputes.

(16) Whoever usurps land and plants in it or builds, it is said to him: Uproot the plant and the building and return the land empty to its owner. If the land is diminished by that uprooting, the owner may hold him liable for the value of the building and the plant uprooted. [Urdu commentary explains the ruling and then hadith proofs.] (a) Narrated Sa'id ibn Zayd from the Prophet (ﷺ): 'Whoever revives dead land, it is his, and there is no right for a wrongful root.' (Sunan Abi Dawud, Tirmidhi) (b) Narrated Rafi' ibn Khudayj: 'Whoever sows in land of a people without their permission, nothing of the crop is for him, and he gets his expenses.' (c) Narrated Yahya ibn 'Urwa from his father: Two men disputed before the Prophet (ﷺ): one had planted palm trees in the other's land. He judged that the land belongs to its owner and ordered the planter to remove his palm trees. The narrator says: 'I saw their roots being struck with axes, and it was a huge palm, until they were removed.' (Abu Dawud, Bayhaqi) Footnotes explain the hadith.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling for a usurper who plants trees or builds on someone else's land. The Hanafi ruling is that he must remove his plants and building, returning the land empty. If removal damages the land (e.g., holes from uprooting), the owner can claim compensation for the value of the removed items (in their reduced state). The text then provides three hadith proofs: (1) 'No right for a wrongful root' indicates that one who plants wrongfully has no claim to the land or plants. (2) The hadith of Rafi' ibn Khudayj: the planter has no right to the crop but only gets his expenses (i.e., cost of seeds/labor). (3) The story of the palm trees: the Prophet ordered the planter to remove his trees, even if it required effort. This establishes the principle that the usurper must clear the land at his own cost.

Key Terms
غرس (ghars): Planting trees or plants; in this context, the act of planting on usurped land.
قلع (qal‘): Uprooting or removing; the required action of the usurper to clear the land.
عرق ظالم (‘irq zālim): A plant or root placed wrongfully on another's land; synonym for usurper's planting.

Option for Owner When Usurper Changes the Item by Dyeing or Mixing
Ruling on owner's choice after usurpation with alteration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two options available to the owner of a usurped item that has been altered, and apply the ruling to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Owner may either claim original value and keep altered item, or claim value and leave it with usurper.

When someone usurps your property and changes it — such as dyeing a cloth or mixing ghee into flour — you are entitled to full compensation for the original value. Islamic law gives you the flexibility to decide what to do with the altered item: you may either take it yourself or leave it with the usurper. This lesson explores this ruling and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act of usurpation and alteration
The usurper took the property and changed it — e.g., cloth dyed red, or sawiq mixed with ghee. Note that the alteration does not destroy the item but transforms it.
2
Recognize the owner's right to full compensation
The owner is entitled to the value of the item before alteration — i.e., the price of a white cloth or the equivalent of unmixed sawiq. This is the foundation of the ruling.
Compensation is for the original, not the altered, value.
3
Distinguish between the two options
Option 1: Owner claims compensation and gives the altered item to the usurper. Option 2: Owner claims compensation and takes the altered item for himself. Both options involve the same compensation amount.
The owner may choose whichever suits him.
4
Apply the rule to similar cases
This principle applies to any usurped item that is altered without being destroyed — e.g., dyeing cloth, mixing foods, painting wood, etc. The owner retains the choice.
If the item is destroyed, different rules of damages (daman) apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۷) ومن غصب ثوبا فصبغه احمر او سويقا فلته بسمن فصاحبه بالخيار ان شاء ضمنه قيمة ثوب ابيض ومثل السويق ودفع اليه المصبوغ والمخلوط وان شاء ضمنه قيمة ثوب ابيض ومثل السويق واخذ المصبوغ والمخلوط. کسی نے کپڑا غصب کیا اور اس کو سرخ رنگ میں رنگ دیا یا ستو غصب کیا اور اس میں گھی ملا دیا تو اس کے مالک کو اختیار ہے اگر چاہے تو اس کو سفید کپڑے کا ضامن بنائے اور ستو کے مثل کا ضامن بنائے اور وہ کپڑا اور ستو غاصب کو دے دے۔ اور اگر چاہے تو دونوں کو لے لے۔
(17) Whoever usurps a cloth and dyes it red, or usurps sawiq (barley meal) and mixes it with ghee, then its owner has the choice: if he wishes, he may hold him liable for the value of a white cloth and the like of the sawiq, and hand over the dyed and mixed to him; or he may hold him liable for the value of a white cloth and the like of the sawiq, and take the dyed and mixed.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This ruling gives the owner of a usurped item that has been altered (dyed or mixed) two options. Option one: The owner can demand compensation for the original value of the item (the cloth's value before dyeing, or the equivalent of the sawiq) and let the usurper keep the altered item. Option two: He can demand compensation for the original value and also take the altered item for himself. In both cases, the owner gets full compensation; the difference is whether he also takes the altered item. The commentary explains that the owner has the choice: either take the changed item and claim the original value, or leave it with the usurper and claim the original value. The ruling applies when the alteration does not destroy the essence but changes it. If the alteration is reversible, similar rules may apply. Note: The owner cannot claim both the item and its original value without giving something; he either takes the item plus compensation for the difference, but here he gets full compensation regardless.

Key Terms
سويق (sawīq): Barley meal or ground grain; a food item that can be mixed with ghee.
فلته (faltah): Mixed (with ghee); from the verb 'falata' meaning to mix or blend.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Claim the value of a white cloth and take the dyed cloth, OR claim the value and leave the dyed cloth with the usurper.
B) Only claim the value of the dyed cloth.
C) Force the usurper to return the cloth in its original condition.
D) Take the dyed cloth without any compensation.

Key Takeaway
The owner of a usurped altered item receives full compensation for the original value and has the choice to either take or leave the altered item.

6 min
Owner's Right of Choice When Usurper Alters Property
Ruling on owner's options when usurper changes property attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two options available to the owner when a usurper alters property by adding an increase in attribute (dyeing or mixing), and apply the ruling to similar cases.

Core Idea
The owner may either take compensation for the original and yield the altered item, or keep it and pay for the increase.

When a usurper dyes a garment or mixes flour with butter, the property has been changed in a way that cannot be easily reversed. The Hanafi school gives the owner a choice between two equitable remedies, ensuring neither party suffers unjust enrichment. This lesson examines the ruling and the hadith evidence that supports it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and alteration
Determine what the usurper did: he took a white garment and dyed it red, or took barley flour and mixed it with clarified butter. The alteration is an increase in attribute (ziyada wasfiyya) that enhances the item's value.
2
State the two options
The owner may either (1) demand compensation for the original item (value of white garment, equivalent of barley flour) and hand the altered item to the usurper, or (2) keep the altered item and pay the usurper the amount of the increase (value of dye, value of clarified butter).
The options are equally valid; the owner chooses.
3
Locate the dalil
Three hadiths are cited: (a) reviving dead land (the planter gets the land but no right for unjust root), (b) sowing without permission (the sower gets his expenses but no crop), (c) the palm trees planted on another's land (the trees must be removed). These analogies support the two-option rule.
All hadiths indicate the usurper's additions are not owned by him in the usurped property.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The hadiths show that the usurper's labour or addition is compensated (he has his expenses) but does not transfer ownership. The owner retains the right to either take the original value (forcing removal of the addition) or keep the addition with compensation.
The principle is that the owner gains no unjust enrichment, nor does the usurper.
5
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools may not allow the owner to keep the item and pay the increase if the alteration is substantial; the Hanafi view is more flexible, permitting the owner to choose either option.
The practical consequence: Hanfi owners have a right others may restrict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن غصب ثوبا فصبغه احمر او سويقا فلته بسمن فصاحبه بالخيار ان شاء ضمنه قيمة ثوب ابيض ومثل السويق وسلمه للغاصب وان شاء اخذهما وضمن ما زاد الصبغ والسمن فيهما. عن سعيد بن زيد عن النبي قال من احيا ارضا ميتة فهى له وليس لعرق ظالم حق. عن رافع بن خديج قال قال رسول الله انه من زرع في أرض قوم بغير اذنهم فليس له من الزرع شيء وله نفقته. عن يحيى بن عروة عن ابيه ان رجلين اختصما الى رسول الله غرس احدهما نخلا في ارض الآخر فقضى لصاحب الارض بارضه وامر صاحب النخل ان يخرج نخله منها قال فلقد رأيتها وانها لتضرب اصولها بالفؤس وانها لنخل عم حتى اخرجت منها.
Whoever usurps a garment and dyes it red, or usurps barley flour and mixes it with clarified butter, then its owner has the option: if he wishes, he may make him liable for the value of a white garment and the equivalent of the barley flour, and hand them over to the usurper; and if he wishes, he may take both and pay what the dye and clarified butter increased in them. From Sa'id ibn Zayd from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: Whoever revives dead land, it is his, and there is no right for an unjust root. From Rafi' ibn Khadij that he said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: Whoever sows in the land of a people without their permission, he has nothing of the crop, and he has his expenses. From Yahya ibn 'Urwa from his father that two men came to the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) with a dispute: one of them had planted palm trees in the other's land. He judged that the land belonged to its owner and ordered the owner of the palm trees to remove his trees from it. He said: I saw them, and indeed they were striking their roots with axes, and they were tall palm trees, until they were removed from it.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage begins a ruling from the Book of Usurpation (Ghasp). The issue: a person usurps a white garment and dyes it red, or usurps barley flour (sawiq) and mixes it with clarified butter (saman), thereby increasing its value. The owner is given two choices: (1) He may demand from the usurper the value of the original white garment and the original barley flour, and hand over the altered items to the usurper. (2) He may keep the altered items, but must pay the usurper the amount of the increase caused by the dye or clarified butter. This is based on fairness and preventing loss to either party. The text then provides three hadith proofs: (a) The hadith of reviving dead land: if someone plants on usurped land, the plantings belong to the usurper but must be removed, analogous to the dye being part of the garment; (b) The hadith of Rafi' ibn Khadij: if someone plants without permission, he gets only his expenses, not the crop—this supports that the usurper gets compensation for his effort (the dye's value) but not the entire enhanced item; (c) The hadith of the palm trees: the Prophet ordered the removal of the trees, showing that the usurper's additions must be undone, but as a rule, the owner can also choose to keep them with compensation. The hadiths together establish the two-option rule.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation: taking another's property without right and without consent, with the intention of ownership or use.
خيار (khiyar): Option: the right to choose between two or more alternatives in a legal transaction.
صبغ (sabgh): Dyeing: applying color to a garment, which is considered an increase in attribute (ziyada wasfiyya).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must take the red garment and pay the dye's value.
B) He may either claim the value of a white garment and give the red one to the usurper, or keep the red garment and pay the increase.
C) He must return the red garment to the usurper and demand nothing.
D) He can only demand compensation for the original garment and cannot keep the altered item.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the owner of usurped property that has been altered has a choice between taking compensation for the original or keeping the altered item and paying for the increase.

Usurper Becomes Owner of Lost Property by Paying Its Value
Ruling that paying the value transfers ownership of the lost usurped item to the usurper

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a usurper becomes owner of a lost item and identify the hadith evidence supporting it.

Core Idea
Paying the value of a lost usurped item transfers ownership to the usurper.

Usurpation (ghasb) is a prohibited act, but what happens if the usurped item is lost and the owner demands its value? This lesson examines the Hanafi position that compensation for the lost item establishes ownership. We will explore the ruling, its proof from the hadith of the sound and broken bowls, and the disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal consequence
The act is usurpation (ghasb) of a specific item (ayn). The consequence is that if the item is lost and the owner is paid its value, the usurper becomes owner.
This is a legal consequence, not a prescribed act.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith of the sound bowl: the Prophet kept the broken bowl after giving a sound one, indicating that compensation transfers ownership.
Hadith reference: Sahih al-Bukhari, no. 248.
3
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's action shows that giving a sound bowl in exchange for keeping the broken one functions as a sale or compensation, transferring ownership of the broken bowl.
The analogy is that the usurper's payment of value transfers ownership of the lost item.
4
Contrast with Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that a prohibited cause (usurpation) cannot effect ownership, so the usurper does not become owner even after payment.
This is a valid ikhtilaf based on different usul principles.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Hanafis prioritize the explicit hadith and the principle that compensation (damān) can transfer ownership, making the position mufta bihi.
The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن غصب عينا فغيَّبها فضمنه المالك قیمتها ملكها الغاصب بالقيمة. والدليل حديث: فدفع القصعة الصحيحة وحبس المكسورة.
And whoever usurps a specific item and then makes it lost, and the owner makes him liable for its value, the usurper owns it by its value. The evidence is the hadith: He gave the sound bowl and kept the broken one.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Book of Usurpation: if a person usurps a specific item (ayn) and then the item is lost (ghayyabaha), and the owner demands and receives the value (qima) from the usurper, then the usurper becomes the owner of that item. This is because the payment of compensation effectively transfers ownership. The proof is the hadith of the sound bowl and the broken bowl: a woman gave the Prophet (peace be upon him) a broken bowl as a gift but he returned it and asked for a sound one; he kept the sound one and returned the broken one, indicating that payment (or exchange) can transfer ownership. This ruling is specific to the Hanafi school; Imam al-Shafi'i (mentioned in the commentary) objects that usurpation is a prohibited cause and thus cannot effect ownership, so the usurper should not become owner even after paying value. However, the Hanafi position relies on the hadith and the principle that compensation brings ownership.

Key Terms
عین (ayn): Specific, identifiable item (as opposed to debt).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The usurper becomes the owner of that item.
B) The owner retains ownership and the usurper simply pays compensation.
C) The item is considered charity.
D) The usurper must also pay a fine.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, compensating the owner for a lost usurped item transfers ownership to the usurper, based on the hadith of the sound and broken bowls.

Owner's Options When Usurper Adds Value
Ruling on owner's options when usurper adds value through dyeing or mixing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two options available to the owner when a usurper adds value to a usurped item, and explain the Hanafi evidence from the hadith of Rafi' ibn Khadij.

Core Idea
The owner may reclaim the item and pay for the added value, or accept compensation for the original item—never unfair loss.

Imagine someone dyes your white garment red without permission, or mixes ghee into your barley flour. Does the garment become his? The Hanafi school balances the rights of both parties, giving the owner two fair options. This lesson explores that ruling and its proof from the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Recognize that the passage deals with a usurper who adds value (e.g., dyeing or mixing) without causing defect. The ruling: the owner has two choices.
The added value must be a legitimate increase, not a damage.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith of Rafi' ibn Khadij about planting on another's land without permission.
Hadith: 'He gets no crop but his expenses.'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith shows that the trespasser is entitled only to his costs (expenses), not the enhanced value. By analogy, the dyer or mixer gets only the cost of the dye/ghee.
The analogy (qiyas) is based on the shared illa of unauthorized work adding value.
4
Note the underlying principle
The principle: neither owner nor usurper should suffer loss; each gets their due as much as possible.
This avoids unjust enrichment on either side.
5
Apply to a similar scenario
Test your understanding: What if the usurper embroiders a scarf? The same two options apply, with compensation for the cost of thread and labor.
The cost of the added material, not the market value increase.

Source Text

The scenario illustrates both options clearly.

Dispute Over Value: Usurper's Statement Accepted with Oath
Ruling that in a dispute over value, the usurper's statement with oath is accepted

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for valuing a lost usurped item and apply the principle that the claimant must prove, the defendant swears.

Core Idea
In value disputes, the usurper's sworn statement stands unless the owner proves a higher value.

This passage addresses a specific procedural scenario in cases of ghasp (usurpation). When the usurped item is lost and the owner and usurper disagree on its value, the default rule is that the usurper's statement is accepted, but only if he takes an oath. This aligns with the broader Islamic evidence principle that the burden of proof lies on the claimant, and the defendant may swear an oath. Understanding this rule helps clarify how courts resolve valuation disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the dispute
The owner claims a higher value for the lost usurped item; the usurper claims a lower value. Both cannot be accepted simultaneously.
2
Determine who is the claimant
The owner seeks to prove a higher value than the usurper admits, so he is the claimant (mudda'i). The usurper is the defendant (mudda'a alayh).
In Hanafi law, the claimant must produce evidence (bayyinah) to support his claim.
3
Apply the evidence rule
If the owner cannot produce evidence for his higher claim, the defendant's (usurper's) statement is accepted, provided he takes an oath (yamin).
The oath affirms the usurper's stated value.
4
State the exception
If the owner does bring valid evidence (e.g., two male witnesses), that evidence prevails and the higher value is enforced.
Evidence overrides the default rule.
5
Conclude the procedural outcome
Thus, the usurper's statement with oath is the default; the owner's evidence is an exception.
This is consistent with the general maxim: 'al-bayyinah 'ala man idda'a wa al-yamin 'ala man ankara' (The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the denier).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۵۶۹] (۱۹) والقول فى القيمة قول الغاصب مع يمينه الا ان يقيم المالك البيئة باكثر من ذلک
اور قول قیمت میں غاصب کی مانی جائے گی قسم کے ساتھ مگر یہ کہ مالک بینہ قائم کر دے اس سے زیادہ کا ۔ ا غصب کی چیز غائب ہو گئی۔ اب مالک اور غاصب کے درمیان اس کی قیمت میں اختلاف ہو گیا تو اگر مالک کے پاس بعینہ ہے تو اسکے ذریعہ زیادہ قیمت کا فیصلہ کیا جائے گا۔ اور اگر اس کے پاس بینہ نہیں ہے تو غاصب کی بات قسم کے ساتھ مانی جائے گی۔ یہاں مالک زیادہ قیمت کا مدعی ہے اور غاصب اس کا منکر ہے اس لئے مدعی کے پاس بینہ نہ ہو تو منکر کی بات قسم کے ساتھ مانی جاتی ہے۔
[1569] (19) And the statement regarding the value is the statement of the usurper with his oath, unless the owner brings evidence for more than that. The ruling on the value is that the statement of the usurper is accepted with his oath, unless the owner brings evidence for more than that. For example, the usurped item became lost. Now there is a dispute between the owner and the usurper regarding its value. If the owner has evidence, then by it a higher value is decided. And if he has no evidence, then the usurper's statement with oath is accepted. Here the owner is the claimant for a higher value and the usurper is the denier, therefore when the claimant has no evidence, the denier's statement with oath is accepted.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This is a procedural ruling in cases of ghasp. When a usurped item is lost and the owner and usurper disagree on its value, the default rule is that the usurper's statement (as the defendant) is accepted provided he takes an oath. However, if the owner can produce evidence (baiyyinah) proving a higher value, then that evidence prevails. This follows the general principle of evidence: the claimant must produce proof, and the defendant swears an oath. Thus, the usurper is considered the defendant regarding the value.

Key Terms
قول (qawl): In legal disputes, 'qawl' refers to the statement of a party that is given weight under certain conditions.
بینہ (bayyinah): Evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two female witnesses, sufficient to prove a claim.
يمين (yamin): Oath taken by the defendant to affirm his statement; it is required for the acceptance of the usurper's statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The owner's statement without oath
B) The usurper's statement with oath
C) The owner's statement with oath
D) Neither; a third party decides

Key Takeaway
When a usurped item is lost and value is disputed, the usurper's sworn statement is accepted unless the owner proves a higher value with evidence.

Item Reappears With Higher Value: No Option for Owner (Case 1)
Ruling when reappeared item's value exceeds compensation paid by owner's means

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a usurped item reappears with higher value after compensation and identify the three scenarios in which the owner loses his option.

Core Idea
The owner's own valuation binds him; he cannot later claim more or reclaim the item.

Imagine you lost a valuable item and accepted compensation from the person who took it. Later, the item reappears and is worth much more. Do you have any legal recourse? Classical Hanafi fiqh addresses this exact scenario, ruling that if the compensation was based on your own statement, evidence, or the usurper's refusal to swear, you have no further claim. This lesson unpacks the three scenarios and the binding legal principle behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal problem
A usurped item was lost, and compensation was paid based on the owner's input. The item later reappears with a higher value. The question is whether the owner can claim more or reclaim the item.
This is a binding situation once the owner's valuation is established.
2
Recognize the three valuation methods
The text lists three ways the value was set: (1) by the owner's own word, (2) by evidence the owner produced, (3) by the usurper's refusal to take an oath (nukul). In each case, the owner participated in determining the value.
The common element is the owner's involvement in setting the compensated amount.
3
Apply the binding principle
The law considers that the owner was satisfied with the compensation amount; therefore, he cannot later claim a higher value or take back the item. The item becomes the property of the usurper.
The principle: the owner's own actions tie his hands.
4
Grasp the rationale
The owner had the opportunity to state a higher value at the time but did not. Allowing him to reopen the case would undermine finality and fairness.
The ruling prevents abuse and ensures settled disputes remain closed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۵۷۰] (۲۰) فاذا ظهرت العين وقيمتها اكثر مما ضمن وقد ضمنها بقول المالک او ببينة اقامها او بنكول الغاصب عن اليمين فلا خيار للمالك وهو للغاصب
پس اگر عین چیز ظاہر ہو جائے اور اس کی قیمت زیادہ ہے اس سے جو ضمان دیا اور حال یہ تھا کہ اس کا ضمان دیا مالک کے کہنے سے یا گواہ قائم کرنے سے غاصب کے قسم کھانے سے انکار کرنے سے تو مالک کو اختیار نہیں ہوگا اور وہ چیز غاصب کی ہوگی۔ اتش المغصوب چیز غائب تھی اس درمیان اس کی قیمت مالک کو دلوادی گئی، بعد میں وہ چیز ظاہر ہوئی اور پتہ چلا کہ اس کی قیمت زیادہ تھی ۔ پس اگر ان تین صورتوں میں قیمت کا فیصلہ کیا گیا تھا تو مالک کو زیادہ قیمت نہیں دلوائی جائے گی۔ اور نہ مالک کو وہ چیز لینے کا اختیار ہوگا۔ مثلا مالک کے کہنے پر کہ اس چیز کی قیمت اتنی ہے اس کو قیمت دلوا دی گئی تھی تو زیادہ قیمت نہیں دلوائی جائے گی۔ خود مالک نے اس کی قیمت اتنی ہی لگائی ہے۔ اس نے اس وقت زیادہ قیمت کیوں نہیں لگائی یہ اس کی غلطی ہے اس لئے زیادہ قیمت نہیں دلوائی جائے گی۔ یا مالک نے اتنی قیمت پر بینہ قائم کر کے فیصلہ کروایا تھا تو چونکہ خود ہی اس نے اسی قیمت پر گواہی دے کر فیصلہ کروایا ہے اس لئے وہ پہلے سے کم قیمت پر راضی ہے۔ اس لئے اس کو زیادہ قیمت نہیں دلوائی جائے گی۔ یا مالک کے پاس بینہ نہیں تھا اس لئے غاصب کو قسم کھانے کے لئے کہا۔ غاصب نے قسم کھانے سے انکار کر دیا جس کی وجہ سے مالک نے جتنی قیمت بتائی اس پر فیصلہ کر دیا گیا تواب مالک کو زیادہ قیمت نہیں دلوائی جائے گی۔ یہ فیصلے مالک کے قیمت بتانے پر ہوئے ہیں اس لئے چیز کی قیمت زیادہ نکل جائے پھر بھی اس کو زیادہ نہیں دلوائی جائے گی (۲) کیونکہ مالک اتنی ہی قیمت پر راضی ہے۔
اصول یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ مالک جتنی قیمت پر پہلے راضی ہو چکا ہو تو اس سے زیادہ نہیں دلوائی جائے گی اور نہ مالک کو وہ چیز واپس لینے کا اختیار ہوگا۔
ان نکول : قسم کھانے سے انکار کرنا۔
[1570] (20) So when the item appears and its value is more than what was paid as compensation, and it was paid as compensation by the word of the owner, or by evidence he produced, or by the usurper's refusal to take the oath, then there is no option for the owner, and it belongs to the usurper. So if the usurped item was lost, in the meantime its value was paid to the owner, later the item appeared and it was found that its value was higher. So if the compensation was decided in these three ways, then the owner will not be given the higher value, nor will the owner have the option to take the item. For example, on the owner's word that the value of the item is such, the compensation was paid; then the higher value will not be given. The owner himself set that value; why did he not set a higher value at that time? This is his mistake, so he will not be given the higher value. Or the owner produced evidence for that value and had the case decided; then since he himself gave testimony for that value and had it decided, he is satisfied with that lower value, so he will not be given the higher value. Or the owner had no evidence, so the usurper was asked to swear an oath; the usurper refused to swear, so the case was decided on whatever value the owner stated; then also the owner will not be given the higher value. These decisions were based on the owner stating the value, so even if the item's value turns out to be higher, he will not be given more because he was satisfied with that value. Principle: This ruling is based on the principle that once the owner has agreed to a certain value, he will not be given more, nor will he have the option to take back the item. inukul: refusal to take an oath.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This ruling addresses three scenarios where the compensation for a lost usurped item was determined based on the owner's input. If the item later reappears and has a higher value, the owner cannot claim the difference or reclaim the item. The three scenarios are: (1) the owner stated the value himself, (2) the owner produced evidence for that value, and (3) the usurper refused to take an oath, so the owner's stated value was accepted. In all cases, the owner is bound by his own valuation or the valuation he participated in establishing. The principle is that the owner's satisfaction with the compensation precludes further claims. The item remains with the usurper. The term inukul (refusal to take oath) is defined.

Key Terms
نکول (nukūl): Refusal of the defendant to take an oath when required, which can lead to a judgment against him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the usurped item reappears with a value higher than the compensation paid, and the compensation was determined based on the owner's statement, what are the owner's options?
A) He can claim the difference in value
B) He can reclaim the item
C) He has no option; the item belongs to the usurper
D) He can choose between the item or extra payment

Key Takeaway
Once the owner's value is accepted and compensation paid, he has no further claim if the item reappears with higher value.

Owner's Option When Compensation Based on Usurper's Oath
Ruling on owner's option when compensation based on usurper's oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that when compensation is based on the usurper's statement with oath, the owner retains an option to either keep the compensation or reclaim the item.

Core Idea
If the usurper's oath sets the compensation, the owner is not bound and may choose the item instead.

Imagine a usurper loses a valuable item and, when questioned, swears to its value. He pays the owner that amount. Later, the item reappears. Is the owner stuck with the compensation? Hanafi fiqh gives the owner a powerful right: the option (khiyar) to keep the money or return it and take back the item. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The compensation for a lost usurped item was determined by the usurper's own statement, which he supported with an oath (yamīn). The owner did not set the value.
This contrasts with the previous ruling where the owner set the value, giving no option.
2
Recognize the owner's option
The text states: 'the owner has the option (khiyār) to...' This option is a legal right granted to the owner because the compensation was not based on his own valuation.
The option here is a khiyār, not a revocation of a contract but a choice in setting the transaction.
3
Determine the two choices
The owner may choose either to keep the compensation (the usurper's payment) or to return the compensation and take back the item.
If the item is worth more than the compensation, the owner would likely choose the item; if less, he might keep the money.
4
Contrast with the case where owner set value
If the owner had set the value (previous ruling [1570]), he would have no option because he agreed to that value. Here, the owner did not agree; the valuation was imposed by the usurper's oath.
This distinction highlights the importance of consent in Hanafi fiqh.
5
Apply the ruling
Whenever a usurper's statement with oath determines compensation, the owner retains the option to rescind. This protects the owner from an unfair valuation.
The ruling applies even if the owner initially accepted the compensation, as long as it was based on the usurper's oath.

Source Text

[۱۱۵۷۱ وان كان ضمنها بقول الغاصب مع يمينه فالمالک بالخيار ان
[1571] And if he had guaranteed it by the statement of the usurper with his oath, then the owner has the option to...
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is incomplete in the current window. It begins the ruling for the scenario where the compensation for the lost usurped item was determined based on the usurper's own statement with his oath. In contrast to the previous ruling where the owner set the value, here the usurper's valuation prevailed. The ruling likely continues to state that the owner has the option either to keep the compensation or to return it and take back the item. This is a standard Hanafi position: when the usurper's statement is accepted (with oath), the owner is not bound because he did not agree to that value. The full ruling would be found on subsequent pages.

Key Terms
خيار (khiyār): Option or choice given to a party in a contract or compensation situation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) No options; the usurper keeps the item
B) The option to keep the compensation or take the item
C) Only the right to claim extra compensation
D) The item is returned to the owner automatically

Key Takeaway
When compensation for a usurped item is based on the usurper's statement with oath, the owner has the option to keep the compensation or reclaim the item.

Statement of Value: Usurper's Word with Oath
Ruling on whose valuation is accepted when property is missing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default rule for valuation disputes in ghasp and identify when the owner's evidence overrides.

Core Idea
In a value dispute over a missing usurped item, the usurper's word with oath is accepted; the owner must prove higher value.

This lesson addresses a key procedural rule in Hanafi fiqh regarding usurpation (ghasp). When a usurped item is lost and the parties disagree on its value, the default is to accept the usurper's statement with his oath, because the owner is the claimant (mudda'i) and bears the burden of proof. The owner can override this by presenting evidence (bayyinah) of a higher value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Consider a missing usurped item where the owner and usurper disagree on its value. The owner claims a higher value, the usurper claims a lower value.
2
Determine who is claimant and who is denier
The owner claims a higher value (plus extra liability) → claimant (mudda'i). The usurper denies the higher value → denier (munkir). In Hanafi procedure, the claimant must provide evidence; the denier's statement with oath is accepted by default.
This is based on the legal maxim: 'Al-bayyinah 'ala man idda'a wa al-yamin 'ala man ankara' (Evidence is on the claimant, oath on the denier).
3
Apply the default rule
Unless the owner brings evidence, the usurper's statement of the value, accompanied by his oath, is legally accepted. The usurper will be liable for that value.
The oath is a required condition for the usurper's statement to be accepted.
4
Identify the exception
If the owner produces valid evidence (bayyinah – typically two male witnesses or equivalent) proving a higher value, that evidence overrides the usurper's statement.
The owner's evidence must be sound and directly establish the higher value.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The usurper's liability is based on the accepted valuation. If the owner proves higher value, the usurper must pay that amount. If not, he pays the lower value he swore to.
This rule prevents the owner from claiming an unsubstantiated high value and protects the usurper from excessive claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول فى القيمة قول الغاصب مع يمينه الا ان يقيم المالك البيئة باكثر من ذلک
And the statement regarding the value is the statement of the usurper with his oath, unless the owner establishes evidence of more than that.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This is a ruling from the chapter of Ghasp regarding a usurped item that is missing. When the owner and usurper disagree on its value, the default is that the usurper's statement is accepted, provided he takes an oath. This is because the owner is the claimant (mudda'i) of a higher value and the usurper is the denier (munkir). According to Hanafi procedural law, the burden of proof lies on the claimant, and in its absence, the denier's statement with oath prevails. If the owner brings evidence (bayyinah) showing a higher value, then that evidence is accepted over the usurper's statement.

Key Terms
القول (al-qawl): The statement that is legally accepted in a dispute.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a usurped item is missing and the owner and usurper disagree on its value, whose statement is accepted by default?
A) The owner's statement
B) The usurper's statement with his oath
C) The value set by a judge
D) The average market value

Key Takeaway
When a usurped item is missing, the usurper's valuation with oath is accepted unless the owner proves a higher value.

Owner's Option When Property Reappears with Higher Value
Rulings on owner's options when property reappears with higher value after compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four scenarios of owner's option when usurped property reappears with higher value, and determine in which scenario the owner retains an option.

Core Idea
If the owner's own statement or evidence set the compensation, no option; if the usurper's oath set it, the owner can reclaim.

This passage from a Hanafi matn addresses a specific situation: a usurped property is missing, compensation is paid, and later the property reappears with a higher value. The ruling distinguishes four ways the compensation was determined, and based on that, the owner either has no option or has the option to keep the compensation or take back the property. Understanding this distinction prevents unfair enrichment and clarifies the binding nature of previous settlements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Ruling
The act is the reappearance of usurped property after compensation has been paid. The ruling concerns the owner's option (khiyar) to either keep the compensation or reclaim the property when its value exceeds the compensation.
The owner's option is not absolute; it depends on how the compensation was determined.
2
Distinguish the Four Scenarios
Compensation may be determined by: (a) the owner's own statement, (b) evidence the owner brought, (c) the usurper's refusal to take an oath (nukul), or (d) the usurper's statement with his oath. Classify each scenario clearly.
Scenarios (a)-(c) share a common feature; scenario (d) is distinct.
3
Determine the Owner's Option in Each Scenario
In scenarios (a), (b), and (c), the owner has no option — the property becomes owned by the usurper. In scenario (d), the owner has the option: either uphold the guarantee or reclaim the property and return the compensation.
The owner's own involvement in setting the value (directly or via proof) precludes a later claim.
4
Identify the Underlying Principle
When the owner was instrumental in setting the compensation (by his own statement, his evidence, or his demand for an oath leading to nukul), he is bound by it. When the compensation was set solely by the usurper's oath (without the owner's input), the owner retains flexibility.
This principle protects against the owner's unilateral change after settlement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاذا ظهرت العين وقيمتها اكثر مما ضمن وقد ضمنها بقول المالك او ببينة اقامها او بنكول الغاصب عن اليمين فلا خيار للمالك وهو للغاصب وان كان ضمنها بقول الغاصب مع يمينه فالمالک بالخيار ان شاء امضى الضمان وان شاء اخذ العين ورد العوض
So if the property appears and its value is more than what was guaranteed, and it was guaranteed by the statement of the owner, or by evidence he established, or by the refusal of the usurper to take an oath, then there is no option for the owner, and it is for the usurper. And if he guaranteed it by the statement of the usurper with his oath, then the owner has the option: if he wishes, he may uphold the guarantee, and if he wishes, he may take the property and return the compensation.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This ruling covers four scenarios after a missing usurped property reappears and is found to be of higher value than the compensation already paid. In three scenarios—when the compensation was determined based on the owner's own statement of value, or based on evidence the owner brought, or based on the usurper's refusal to take an oath—the owner has no option to demand more or take back the property. The property becomes owned by the usurper because the owner implicitly agreed to that value. In the fourth scenario, when the compensation was based on the usurper's statement with his oath (because the owner lacked evidence), the owner has the option to either keep the compensation and let the usurper keep the property, or take back the property and return the compensation. This distinction is because in the first three cases, the owner was instrumental in determining the value; in the fourth, it was the usurper's statement that set the value.

Key Terms
نكول (nukul): Refusal of the defendant to take an oath after the plaintiff demands it; results in a judgment against the defendant.
خيار (khiyar): Option or choice given to a party in a contract or liability situation.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee, liability, or compensation for loss or damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which scenario does the owner have the option to either keep the compensation or take back the property?
A) When compensation was based on the owner's own statement
B) When compensation was based on evidence the owner brought
C) When compensation was based on the usurper's refusal to take an oath
D) When compensation was based on the usurper's statement with his oath

Key Takeaway
The owner's option to reclaim reappeared property depends on how the compensation was determined: if by his own input (statement, evidence, or via nukul), he is bound; if by the usurper's oath alone, he may choose.
