Offspring and Increase of Usurped Property: Trust, Not Usurped
Ruling on status of offspring and increase of usurped property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that offspring and increase of usurped property are a trust, and identify the conditions that create liability for the usurper.

Core Idea
Natural increase of a usurped item is a trust, not usurped; liability arises only by transgression or refusal to return.

When property is wrongfully taken (ghasp), the usurper is liable for the original item. But what about its natural offspring or growth? In this lesson, we examine a critical Hanafi ruling: the increase itself is not usurped but is held as a trust (amanah). This distinction protects the usurper from accidental loss unless they transgress or refuse to return it upon demand.

Guided Path
1
Identify the items
The ruling mentions offspring of a female slave, natural growth, and fruit of a garden. These are examples of natural increase that arise from the usurped property.
The increase must be natural, not produced by the usurper's labor.
2
Understand default status
These items are declared a trust (amanah) in the usurper's hand. This means the usurper is not liable for accidental loss, unlike the original usurped property which is guaranteed.
Trust status is the default for natural increase.
3
Identify conditions for liability
The usurper becomes liable if he commits ta'addi (transgression) such as using or damaging the increase, or if the owner demands return and the usurper refuses (man').
Refusal after demand converts trust into guarantee.
4
Recognize the rationale
The increase is a new property that came into existence during the usurper's possession. It is not subject to the original wrongful seizure, hence it is treated as a trust.
This prevents unfair liability for accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وولد المغصوبة ونماؤها وثمرة البستان المغصوب امانة في يد الغاصب ان هلک فی یده فلا ضمان علیه الا ان يتعدى فيها او يطلبها مالكها فيمنعها اياه
And the offspring of the usurped (female slave) and its increase and the fruit of the usurped garden are a trust in the hand of the usurper. If it perishes in his hand, there is no guarantee upon him unless he transgresses in it or its owner requests it and he prevents him from it.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This ruling clarifies that the natural growth or produce of a usurped item—such as a child born to a usurped slave-girl, an increase in value, or fruit from a usurped garden—is not itself usurped. Rather, it is considered a trust (amanah) in the usurper's possession. As a trust, the usurper is not liable for its loss unless he commits transgression (ta'addi) or after the owner demands it, he refuses to hand it over. This is because usurpation (ghasp) is defined as unjust seizure of property, which applies only to the original item, not to what arises from it naturally. The increase is a new property that came into existence in the usurper's hands, so its ruling is that of a trust.

Key Terms
امانة (amanah): A trust; property held by someone without liability for loss unless caused by negligence or transgression.
تعدى (ta'addi): Transgression or exceeding proper bounds; here, causing harm or misuse of the trust property.
منع (man'): Prevention or refusal to return the property after demand; converts trust into liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition does the usurper become liable for the offspring of a usurped female slave?
A) If the offspring perishes accidentally
B) If the usurper commits transgression or refuses to return it upon demand
C) The usurper is always liable for the offspring
D) Only if the owner proves negligence

Key Takeaway
Natural increase of usurped property is a trust, not usurped; the usurper is only liable if he commits transgression or refuses to return upon demand.

Defect from Childbirth of Usurped Slave Girl
Ruling on compensation for defect in usurped slave girl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the liability for the defect caused by childbirth in a usurped slave girl and apply the condition under which the child’s value compensates the deficiency.

Core Idea
The child’s value can offset the defect if sufficient.

This lesson examines a specific compensation mechanism in Hanafi fiqh: when a usurped female slave gives birth, the owner may have suffered a loss from the mother’s decreased value. The child, however, is an asset. The ruling balances these by allowing the child’s value to offset the loss, provided it is enough.

Guided Path
1
Identify the loss and liable party
Recognize that any decrease in the slave girl's value due to childbirth is a defect (nuqsan) that the usurper is responsible for (daman).
The defect is not physical injury but market value reduction.
2
Determine the condition for compensation
Check whether the value of the newborn child (al- walad) is sufficient to cover the defect (wafa' bihi).
If the child's value equals or exceeds the defect, the condition is met.
3
Apply the compensation mechanism
When the condition holds, the child itself makes up for the deficiency (jabr al-nuqsan bi al-walad). The usurper gives the child to the owner and owes no cash.
The child is already rightfully the owner's property, so this is an offset in kind.
4
Consider the shortfall scenario
If the child's value is insufficient, the usurper must pay the remaining shortfall in cash.
This follows from the general principle that the usurper must make good all losses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما نقصت الجارية بالولادة فهو في ضمان الغاصب فان كان فى قيمة الولد وفاء به جبر النقصان بالولد
And whatever the slave girl decreased due to childbirth is in the guarantee of the usurper. So if in the value of the child there is sufficient, then the deficiency is compensated by the child.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
When a usurped female slave gives birth while in the usurper's possession, she may suffer a decrease in value due to the effects of childbirth. This decrease is a loss that must be made good by the usurper, as he is responsible for the property. However, the child born to her is a valuable asset. If the value of the child is enough to cover the decrease, then the deficiency is considered compensated by the child itself. The usurper can give the child to the owner to offset the loss. This ruling balances the interests: the owner gets the child (which is rightfully his) and the defect is compensated.

Key Terms
نقصان (nuqsan): Decrease in value or defect; here, the reduction in the slave girl's market value due to childbirth.
جبر (jabr): Compensation or offsetting a loss; here, the child's value makes up for the defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How is the defect in a usurped slave girl due to childbirth compensated?
A) The usurper pays cash equal to the defect
B) The child's value offsets the defect if sufficient
C) The usurper must return both the slave girl and the child without compensation
D) No compensation is needed

Key Takeaway
The defect from childbirth is guaranteed by the usurper, but if the child's value is sufficient, it compensates the deficiency.

Increase of Usurped Property as Amanah
Ruling that offspring and produce of usurped property are held as trust (amanah) by the usurper

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the increase (namā’) of usurped property and identify the two conditions that trigger liability (ḍamān) for the usurper.

Core Idea
Increase of usurped property is a trust; the usurper is not liable unless he commits transgression or refuses to return it after demand.

In Hanafi fiqh, when property is usurped (ghaṣb), the original item is subject to strict liability. But what about its natural increase—offspring, fruit, or growth? This lesson examines a key ruling from the Book of Usurpation that treats the increase as a trust (amānah) in the hands of the usurper, protecting him from liability unless he crosses specific boundaries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on increase
Read the matn: offspring and produce are a trust in the usurper's hand. Understand that the increase is not considered part of the usurped property for liability purposes.
This is a separate legal status from the original.
2
Note the default rule: no liability
If the increase perishes in the usurper's hand without his fault, he is not liable to compensate the owner.
The trust rule protects the usurper from liability for accidental loss.
3
Identify the two exceptions
Liability arises if (1) the usurper commits transgression (ta‘addī) against the increase, or (2) the owner demands the increase and the usurper refuses to return it.
In the second case, the status converts from trust to de facto usurpation.
4
Apply the principle to a scenario
Consider a usurped garden with fruit. The fruit is a trust. If it rots naturally, no liability. If the usurper picks and sells it (transgression), he must compensate. If the owner asks for it and the usurper says no, then the fruit becomes like a usurped item and he is liable even for accidental loss.
Practice applying both exceptions.

Source Text

اصول بڑھوتری غاصب کے ہاتھ میں امانت ہوتی ہے۔
نماء : بڑھوتری ۔
And the child of a usurped female slave and its increase and the fruit of a usurped garden are a trust in the hand of the usurper. If it perishes in his hand, there is no guarantee upon him except that he commits transgression regarding them or the owner demands them from him and he withholds them.
The child of the usurped item, its increase, and the fruit of the usurped garden are in the hand of the usurper as a trust. If it perishes in his hand, there is no guarantee upon him unless he commits transgression or the owner demands it and he does not give it.
In this statement there are two points. One: when a property is usurped, it is usurped, but the increase that occurred in the hand of the usurper, such as a child or fruit, is a trust. Because usurpation means ousting the owner, and this meaning applies to the original item. The increase was not in the owner's possession; it arose in the usurper's hand. So the increase is a trust. The rule for a trust is that if it perishes without transgression, there is no guarantee. Second: if the owner demands the increase and the usurper refuses, then it is no longer a trust but becomes like a usurped item due to the refusal. Thus, if it perishes even without transgression, the usurper must compensate.
Principle: increase in the usurper's hand is a trust.
Increase: growth.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Book of Usurpation (Ghasp). The matn states that the offspring, increase, and fruit of a usurped property are held as a trust (amanah) in the hands of the usurper. The commentary expands this by clarifying the distinction between the original usurped item and its natural growth. The original item is subject to the strict rules of ghasp — the usurper is liable for it. However, the increase (birth, fruit, growth) arises after the usurpation and was never in the owner's possession. Therefore, its legal status is that of a trust: if it is destroyed without any fault or transgression, the usurper is not liable. But liability arises if the usurper commits transgression (deliberate damage) or if the owner demands the item and the usurper refuses to return it. In the latter case, the refusal converts the trust into a de facto usurpation, making the usurper liable even for accidental loss. The passage concludes by reinforcing the principle that increase in the usurper's hand is a trust.

Key Terms
ولد المغصوبة (walad al-maghsūbah): The child born to a usurped female slave after the usurpation.
نماء (namā’): Natural increase or growth that occurs in the property after usurpation, such as offspring of an animal or fruit of a tree.
امانة (amānah): A trust held without ownership; the holder is not liable for loss unless negligent or transgressive.
ضمان (ḍamān): Legal liability to compensate for loss or damage.
تعدى (ta‘addī): Transgression or misuse beyond the permissible; here, any action that causes damage or exceeds the bounds of trust.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the usurper become liable for the increase (namā’) of a usurped property?
A) Always, because it is part of the usurped property.
B) Only if he commits transgression (ta‘addī) or refuses to return it after demand.
C) Never, because increase is always a trust.
D) Only if the increase perishes intentionally.

Key Takeaway
The increase of usurped property is a trust in the usurper's hand; liability only arises if the usurper commits transgression or refuses to return it after the owner's demand.

Compensating Decrease in Usurped Slave-Girl Due to Childbirth
Ruling that the decrease in value of a usurped female slave due to childbirth is covered by the value of the child.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a benefit arising from the same event offsets the loss, and calculate the usurper's liability in cases of usurped property with concurrent benefits and losses.

Core Idea
When a usurped slave-girl gives birth, the child's value offsets the decrease, eliminating the usurper's liability if sufficient.

This lesson examines a specific Hanafi ruling on damages for usurpation (ghasb) when the very act that caused loss also generated a benefit for the owner. A usurper takes a female slave who later gives birth. Childbirth reduces the slave's market value, but the newborn child is an asset. The Hanafi school balances these two outcomes: the loss is compensated by the gain, so the usurper only pays if the child's value falls short.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the loss
The usurper's wrongful possession led to childbirth, which caused a decrease in the slave-girl's value. This loss is initially under the usurper's guarantee.
2
Identify the benefit
The child born is a valuable asset that belongs to the owner. The child's value is a benefit arising from the same event (the usurpation and childbirth).
3
Apply the principle of jabr (offsetting)
The benefit (child's value) compensates for the harm (decrease in value). If the child's value covers the loss, the usurper's liability drops entirely. If not, he pays the remaining difference.
This is derived from the broader Hanafi maxim: 'When benefit and harm coexist, harm is offset by benefit.'
4
State the outcome
The usurper's liability for the decrease is eliminated if the child's value equals or exceeds the loss. The owner keeps the child and the decreased slave, with no further compensation.

Source Text

باندی کی ولادت میں نفع بھی ہے کہ مالک کا ایک غلام بڑھ گیا لیکن ولادت کی وجہ سے باندی میں نقصان بھی ہو گیا تو چونکہ ایک ہی چیز سے فائدہ اور نقصان دونوں ہیں اس لئے بچے کی قیمت سے باندی کا نقصان پورا کیا جائے گا۔ اور جب پورا ہو جائے تو غاصب سے نقصان کا ضمان ساقط ہو جائے گا۔
اصول یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ فائدہ اور نقصان دونوں ہوں تو نقصان کو فائدہ سے پورا کر دیا جائے گا اور غاصب پر نقصان کا ضمان نہیں ہوگا۔
وفاء : (پورا ہونا، جبر : نقصان کی مکافات کرنا۔
And what the slave-girl lost (in value) due to childbirth is under the guarantee of the usurper. If the value of the child covers it, the loss is compensated by the child, and the guarantee falls from the usurper.
In the childbirth of the slave-girl there is a benefit: the owner gained a slave, but the childbirth caused a loss in the slave-girl's value. Since both benefit and loss arise from the same act, the loss from the slave-girl is compensated by the value of the child. When it is fully compensated, the usurper's liability for the loss is dropped.
The principle of this issue is that when both benefit and loss exist, the loss is compensated by the benefit, and the usurper is not liable for the loss.
Wafā’: to cover. Jabr: to compensate for loss.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage deals with a specific case: a usurper has taken a female slave (jāriya), and she gives birth while in his possession. Childbirth typically decreases the market value of the slave-girl (due to physical changes). The ruling holds the usurper liable for this decrease. However, the child born is also valuable property belonging to the owner. Since the child is a benefit arising from the same event (the usurpation), the value of the child is used to offset the decrease. If the child's value is sufficient to cover the loss, then the usurper's liability for the decrease is extinguished. The commentary derives a broader principle: when an act produces both benefit and harm, the harm is compensated by the benefit, relieving the injuring party from further liability.

Key Terms
جارية (jāriya): A female slave; the object of usurpation in this ruling.
ولادة (wilādah): Childbirth; the event that causes a decrease in value of the slave-girl.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability for compensation; here it refers to the obligation to make good the decrease in value.
جبر (jabr): Compensation or offsetting of loss; here the child's value 'jabra' the decrease.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The child's value compensates the decrease, and the usurper's liability is removed.
C) The usurper keeps the child and pays nothing.
D) The owner must choose between the child and compensation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school applies the principle of jabr: benefit arising from the same event offsets the loss, potentially eliminating liability.

Usurper Not Liable for Usufruct Unless Property Depreciates
Ruling with expansion and evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a usurper's liability for usufruct and apply the condition of physical decrease to determine compensation.

Core Idea
A usurper owes nothing for using property unless the property physically depreciates — usufruct alone is not guaranteed.

This lesson examines a distinctive Hanafi position: the usurper (ghāṣib) is not liable for the usufruct (manāfi‘) of the usurped property, even if he uses it. Compensation is only required if the use causes a decrease in the property's value. The passage provides the rule, a clarifying example, the legal reasoning based on the nature of usufruct, and supporting evidence from the practice of Sayyiduna Ali (RA).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the first sentence of the matn. It states that the usurper is not liable for the usufruct unless the property decreases due to his use.
The word 'except' (إلا) establishes the condition for liability.
2
Understand the Example
The commentary provides a concrete scenario: a usurper who keeps a house for a month without living in it or renting it owes no rental value. If his living causes damage, he must compensate for that damage.
The distinction is between benefit (usufruct) and physical harm (nuqsān).
3
Analyze the Legal Reasoning
The text explains that usufruct is not considered guaranteed property (muttaqawwim) unless it is attached to a lease contract (ijārah). Since no contract exists, the usufruct has no compensable value.
This is a core Hanafi principle: manāfi‘ are not māl without a contract.
4
Examine the Evidence from Ali (RA)
The athar from Sayyiduna Ali (RA) is presented: in a case of a mis-sold slave-girl, Ali ordered return of the property and recovery of the price, but did not impose rent for the period of use.
This supports the rule that usufruct alone does not create liability.
5
Apply the Rule to New Cases
Given a scenario where a person uses another's property without permission, determine whether physical decrease occurred. If no damage, no compensation for use; if damage, compensate only for the decrease.
The presence of a contract changes the ruling entirely.

Source Text

ولا يضمن الغاصب منافع ما غصبه الا ان ينقص باستعماله فيغرم النقصان
نہیں ضامن ہوگا غاصب اس منافع کے جن کو غصب کیا مگر یہ کہ اس کے استعمال سے نقص پیدا ہو جائے تو نقصان کا تاوان دے گا۔
تشریح مثلا گھر غصب کیا اور ایک مہینے تک اپنے پاس رکھے رہانہ خود اس میں رہا اور نہ کسی اور کور نے دیا تو ایک مہینے کی رہائش کے منافع کی جو اجرت ہوگی غاصب اس کا ضامن نہیں ہوگا۔ ہاں ! غاصب کے رہنے کی وجہ سے گھر میں کوئی نقص پیدا ہوتا تو غاصب کو نقصان کا تاوان دینا پڑتا۔
جب تک کہ منافع عقدا جرت نہ ہو ہمارے یہاں وہ متقوم نہیں ہے۔ اور یہاں عقد جرت ہوا نہیں اس لئے یہ متقوم نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے غاصب پر اس کا ضمان لازم نہیں ہوگا۔ ہاں ! رہنے کی وجہ سے گھر کا نقصان ہو جائے تو چونکہ مین چیز کو نقصان کیا اور ہلاک کیا اس لئے اس کی قیمت دینی ہوگی (۲) حضرت علی کے اثر سے پتہ چلتا ہے کہ منافع کی قیمت لازم نہیں ہوگی۔ عن عامر الشعبي في رجل وجد جاريته في يد رجل قد ولدت منه فاقام البينة انها جاريته واقام الذى فى يده الجارية البينة انه اشتراها قال فقال على ياخذ صاحب الجارية جاريته ويوخذ البائع بالخلاص، قال سمعت الشعبي يقول ليس الخلاص بشيء من باع مالا یملک فهو لصاحبه ويتبع المشترى البائع بما اعطاه وليس على البائع اكثر من ان يرد ما اخذ ولا يوخذ غيره (الف) ( سنن للبیہقی ، باب من غصب جاریۃ فہا تھا ثم جاء رب الجاریة ، ج سادس ، ۱۶۶ نمبر ۱۱۵۴۸) اس اثر میں حضرت علی نے مالک کی طرف صرف باندی لوٹانے کا حکم دیا اور مشتری کو کہا کہ بائع کو جتنی قیمت دی ہے وہ واپس لے لے۔ لیکن مشتری نے باندی سے جو فائدہ اٹھایا ہے اس کی کوئی اجرت مشتری پر لازم نہیں کی۔ جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ منافع کی جب تک اجرت متعین نہ کرے اس وقت تک اس کی اجزت لازم حاشیہ : (الف) حضرت عامر شعمی نے فرمایا ایک آدمی نے اپنی باندی ایک آدمی کے پاس پائی ۔ اس نے اس سے بچہ دیا تھا۔ پس اس نے بعینہ قائم کیا کہ اس کی باندی ہے۔ اور جس کے قبضے میں باندی تھی اس نے بینہ قائم کیا کہ اس نے اس کو خریدا ہے۔ پس حضرت علیؓ نے فرمایا کہ باندی والا باندی لے گا اور بائع سے بیچ توڑنے کے لئے کہا جائے گا۔ شیمی سے سنا وہ فرماتے تھے خلاص کوئی چیز نہیں ہے۔ کسی نے کوئی ایسی چیز بیچی جو اس کی نہیں ہے تو وہ اس کے مالک کے لئے ہوگی۔ اور مشتری بائع سے اتنا لے گا جتنا اس کو دیا ہے۔ اور بائع پر اس سے زیادہ لوٹانا ضروری نہیں ہے جتنا لیا ہے۔ اور نہ اس کے علاوہ لے سکتا ہے۔
And the usurper is not liable for the usufruct of what he usurped unless it decreases by his use, then he pays the compensation for the decrease.
The usurper is not liable for the usufructs that he usurped except if use causes a loss, then he pays compensation for the loss.
Explanation: For example, he usurps a house and keeps it for a month, neither living in it nor renting it to anyone; then he is not liable for the rental value of the usufruct of residence. However, if his living in it causes any loss to the house, he must pay compensation for the loss.
As long as the usufruct is not contracted for a wage (ijārah), in our (Hanafi) school it is not considered a guaranteed property (muttaqawwim). Here no contract of ijārah occurred, so it is not muttaqawwim. Therefore, the usurper is not liable for it. However, if use causes damage to the house, since he damaged the actual property, he must pay its value.
(2) The effect of Sayyiduna Ali (RA) indicates that the value of usufruct is not obligatory. From Amir al-Sha’bi concerning a man who found his slave-girl in the hand of another man who had a child from her. He established proof that she was his slave-girl, and the one in whose hand she was established proof that he had bought her. He said: Ali said: The owner of the slave-girl takes his slave-girl, and the seller is forced to rescind (the sale). He said: I heard al-Sha’bi say: al-khalās (rescission) is nothing. Whoever sells what he does not own, it is for its owner. The buyer follows the seller for what he gave him. The seller is not obliged to return more than what he took, and nothing else is taken from him.
In this effect, Ali ordered only the return of the slave-girl to the owner and told the buyer to take back the price from the seller. He did not impose any rent on the buyer for the benefit he had from the slave-girl. This shows that usufruct is not obligatory as long as a wage is not specified.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This is the third ruling on the same topic. The matn states a key Hanafi principle: the usurper is not liable for the usufruct (manāfi‘) of the usurped property, even if he uses it, unless the use causes some physical decrease or damage to the property itself. The commentary explains with an example: usurping a house and not using it results in no liability for rental value. But if living in the house causes wear and tear, the usurper must compensate for that damage. The reasoning provided is that usufruct is not considered 'muttaqawwim' (guaranteed property) unless it is subject to a specific contract of ijārah (hire). Since no such contract exists here, the usufruct has no legal value in terms of liability. The commentary then brings an athar (effect) from Sayyiduna Ali (RA) as evidence. In a case where a man bought a slave-girl who was later proven to belong to another, Ali ruled that the buyer must return the slave-girl to her owner and can only recover the price from the seller; he did not charge the buyer rent for the time he used her. This supports the ruling that usufruct is not compensable unless there is a specific agreement or damage to the property.

Key Terms
منافع (manāfi‘): Usufruct; the benefits or use value derived from a property, such as residence or labor.
نقصان (nuqsān): Decrease or damage to the physical property itself, as opposed to usufruct.
متقوم (muttaqawwim): Property that has legal value and is guaranteed under tort law; usufruct without a lease contract is not muttaqawwim.
عقد جرت (aqd ijārah): A lease or hire contract that specifies a rental payment for usufruct.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, is a usurper liable for the rental value of a house he lived in for a month?
A) Yes, because he benefited from the house.
B) No, because usufruct is not guaranteed without a contract or physical damage.
C) Yes, because he committed a sin.
D) No, only if he rented it out.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a usurper is not liable for usufruct without physical damage or a specific contract; the mere benefit of use does not create a financial obligation.

Liability for Usufruct in Usurpation
Ruling that usurper owes nothing for usufruct unless property damaged

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on usurper's liability for usufruct, identify the condition that triggers liability, and explain the proof from the athar of Ali.

Core Idea
Usurper owes nothing for usufruct unless his use damages the property — benefit without contract is not compensable.

In Hanafi fiqh, the wrongful taking of property (ghasb) raises the question of what must be compensated. This lesson examines a key ruling: the usurper is not liable for the beneficial use (usufruct) of the property unless his actual usage causes a decrease in its substance. We will explore the underlying principle — that usufruct is not legally valuable (muqawwam) without a contract — and the prophetic practice (athar) that supports this position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling from the matn
Read the base text: 'لا يضمن الغاصب منافع ما غصبه الا ان ينقص باستعماله فيغرم النقصان' — the usurper is not liable for usufructs unless his use causes a decrease, in which case he pays for the decrease.
This is the core legal statement.
2
Understand the principle of muqawwam
The commentary explains that usufruct (manafi') is not considered legally valuable property (muqawwam) unless it is contracted as a hire (ijarah). Without a contract, it has no compensable value.
This is a distinctive Hanafi principle.
3
Examine the proof from Ali's athar
The author cites the athar from Amir al-Sha'bi: Ali (ra) ruled that a buyer who unknowingly purchased a stolen slave girl must return her to the owner but was not charged for her service (usufruct) during his possession.
This demonstrates that pure usufruct without a contract creates no liability.
4
Apply the ruling to examples
Consider: if a house is usurped but not used, no rent is owed. If use causes damage (e.g., a broken window), compensation is required only for the damage.
Contrast with a case where the house is rented out by the usurper — different rules apply because a contract exists.

And his liability drops from the usurper [1574 (24)] The usurper is not liable for the usufructs of what he usurped unless there occurs a decrease due to use, then he pays compensation for the decrease. [Urdu commentary: So benefit and harm both exist; therefore the loss of the slave girl is compensated by the child's value. And when it is fully compensated, the liability for loss drops from the usurper. The principle: this issue is based on the principle that when both benefit and harm exist, the loss is compensated by the benefit, and the usurper will not be liable for the loss. Wafa: (fulfillment), Jabr: compensating the loss. (1574) (24) The usurper will not be liable for the usufructs he usurped except if due to use a deficiency occurs, then he will pay compensation for the deficiency. Explanation: For example, he usurped a house and kept it for a month - he did not live in it himself nor let anyone else use it - then he is not liable for the rental value of one month's usufruct. Yes! If due to his staying in it a deficiency occurs, then the usurper must pay compensation for the deficiency. As long as usufruct is not contracted as a hire, according to us it is not muqawwam (valuable). And here no hire contract occurred, so it is not muqawwam. Therefore liability for it is not necessary upon the usurper. Yes! If due to staying, the house is damaged, since he damaged and destroyed the very property, he must pay its value. (2) From the trace of Ali it is understood that the value of usufruct is not necessary. From Amir al-Sha'bi concerning a man who found his slave girl in the hand of another man who had a child from her; he established proof that she is his slave girl, and the one in whose hand she was established proof that he bought her. He (Ali) said: The owner of the slave girl takes his slave girl, and the seller is taken to annul the sale. I heard al-Sha'bi say: Annulment (al-khalas) is nothing. Whoever sells something he does not own, it is for its owner, and the buyer follows the seller for what he gave, and the seller is only required to return what he took, and nothing else is taken from him. (a) (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, chapter on whoever usurps a slave girl and she gives birth then the owner of the slave girl comes, vol. 6, p. 166, no. 11548) In this trace, Ali ordered only returning the slave girl to the owner, and told the buyer to take back from the seller whatever price he gave. But he did not impose any rental upon the buyer for the benefit he took from the slave girl. This shows that the value of usufruct is not necessary as long as no hire is specified. Footnote: (a) Amir al-Sha'bi said: A man found his slave girl with a man who had a child from her. Then he established proof that she is his slave girl, and the one in whose hand she was established proof that he bought her. Then Ali said: The owner of the slave girl takes his slave girl, and the seller is commanded to annul (the sale). I heard al-Sha'bi say: Annulment is nothing. Whoever sold something he does not own, it is for its owner, and the buyer will take from the seller what he gave, and the seller is only required to return what he took, and nothing else is taken from him.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Kitab al-Ghasb concerning the liability of a usurper for the usufruct of the usurped property. The base matn states clearly: the usurper is not liable for the usufructs (manafi') of what he usurped, unless his actual use caused a decrease (nuqs) in the property itself—in which case he must pay compensation for that decrease. The commentary (Sharh) expands by explaining the underlying principle (asl): when both benefit (faa'idah) and harm (nuqsaan) are present, the harm is offset by the benefit. For example, if a person usurps a house and does not use it, he owes no rent. But if his staying causes wear and tear, he pays for that. The commentator clarifies that usufruct is not muqawwam (legally valuable as a property) until it is contracted as a hire (ijarah). Since no hire contract exists, the usufruct has no tangible value in law. The proof (dalil) is an athar from Ali (radiyallahu 'anhu) reported by Amir al-Sha'bi: when a sold slave girl was found to belong to another, Ali ruled that the seller must annul the sale and return the price, but did not charge the buyer for the usufruct of the slave girl. This demonstrates that pure usufruct without a prior contract does not create liability. The Hanafi position is that the usurper's liability is only for the 'ayn (substance) if it is destroyed or diminished; the usufruct as such is not compensated unless it falls under a contractual obligation.

Key Terms
منافع (manāfiʿ): Usufructs or beneficial uses of property, such as housing or transportation.
متقوم (mutaqawwim): Property that is legally valuable and compensable in Islamic law.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability or guarantee to compensate for loss or damage.
غصب (ghaṣb): Usurpation: wrongful possession of another's property without right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, when is a usurper liable for the usufruct of usurped property?
A) Always, because any use without permission is unlawful.
B) Only if the usufruct was previously contracted as a hire (ijarah).
C) Only if his use caused a decrease (nuqsan) in the property.
D) Never, because usufruct is never compensable.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the usurper is not liable for the usufruct of usurped property unless his use causes a decrease in the property's value; this is based on the principle that usufruct is not muqawwam without a contract and is supported by the athar of Ali.

Liability for Destroying a Dhimmi's Wine or Pig
Ruling with proof on damages for destroying prohibited items of dhimmi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for destroying a dhimmi's wine or pig and explain the proof from hadith.

Core Idea
A Muslim must pay for destroying a dhimmi's wine or pig because they are valuable property for the dhimmi.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from Kitab al-Ghasb regarding liability for destroying prohibited items (wine and pig) belonging to a dhimmi versus a Muslim. The Hanafi school distinguishes based on whether the item is considered valuable property for its owner. We will explore the ruling, its legal reasoning, and the prophetic proofs that support it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is a Muslim destroying the wine or pig of a dhimmi. The ruling is that the Muslim is liable for the value of the item.
The ruling is clear: 'اذا استهلك المسلم خمر الذمي ضمن قيمتها' (if a Muslim destroys the dhimmi's wine, he is liable for its value).
2
Understand the basis of liability
Liability depends on whether the item is considered valuable property (maal mutaqawwim) for the owner. For a dhimmi, wine and pig are valuable because they are permitted for them. For a Muslim, they are not valuable.
Wine is dhawat al-amthal (standard measure) but since a Muslim cannot buy it, its value is paid. Pig is dhawat al-qeem (value-based).
3
Examine the proofs
Two hadith are cited: (1) The Prophet ordered to break pots of donkey meat, indicating no liability for destroying unlawful items. (2) Anas broke wine jars after prohibition with no compensation. These show that destroying prohibited items does not incur liability, but the distinction is the owner's status.
The hadith are used to support the idea that liability only attaches when the item is valuable for its owner.
4
Compare with the Muslim case
If the owner is Muslim, there is no liability because wine and pig are not valuable property for Muslims. The same damages would not warrant compensation.
The text explicitly states: 'وان استهلكهما المسلم المسلم لم يضمن' (if a Muslim destroys them from a Muslim, he is not liable).
5
Note the cross-madhhab dispute
Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, holding that wine and pig are never valuable property for anyone, so no liability even for a dhimmi. The Hanafi position is relied upon for fatwa.
This ikhtilaf is legitimate; the Hanafi view is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الغضب
[۱۵۷۵ (۲۵) واذا استهلك المسلم خمر الذمی او خنزیره ضمن قيمتها وان استهلكهما المسلم المسلم لم يضمن.
نہیں ہوتی ہے۔ [۱۵۷۵] (۲۵) مسلمان نے ذمی کے شراب کو یا اس کے سور کو ہلاک کر دیا تو دونوں کی قیمت کا ضامن ہوگا۔ اور اگر ان دونوں کو مسلمان نے مسلمان کا ہلاک کیا تو ضامن نہیں ہوگا۔
ذمی کے حق میں شراب اور سور قیمتی چیز ہیں اس لئے مسلمان نے جب ان کو ضائع کیا تو ان کا ضمان دینا ہوگا۔ البتہ شراب ذواۃ الامثال ہے لیکن مسلمان اس کو خرید نہیں سکتا اس لئے اس کی بھی قیمت ادا کرے گا۔ اور سور ذواۃ القیم ہے ہی اس لئے اس کی تو قیمت دے گا۔ اور مسلمان نے مسلمان کے شراب یا سور کو ہلاک کر دیا تو ان کا ضمان نہیں ہے۔
الله
کیونکہ مسلمان کے حق میں دونوں چیزیں قیمتی نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے ان کو ہلاک کرنے کی وجہ سے اس پر کوئی ضمان لازم نہیں ہوگا (۲) حدیث میں ہے کہ ان چیزوں کو توڑا ہے بلکہ توڑنے کا حکم دیا۔ عن سلمة بن اکوع ان النبی الله رای نیرانا توقد يوم خیبر قال علام توقد هذه النيران قالوا على الحمر الانيسة قال اكسروها وهريقوها (الف) ( بخاری شریف، باب هل تكسر الدنان التی فيها الخمر او تحرق الزقاق ص ۳۳۶ نمبر ۲۴۷۷) اس حدیث میں گدھے کے گوشت کو پھینکنے اور اس کے برتن کو توڑنے کا حکم دیا جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ ناجائز چیزوں کو توڑنے کا ضمان نہیں ہے (۳) عن انس بن مالک قال كنت اسقى ابا عبيدة وابا طلحة وابي بن كعب شرابا من فضيح وتمر فجائهم آت فقال ان الخمر قد حرمت فقال ابو طلحة يا انس قم الى هذه الجرار فاكسرها
قال انس فقمت الى مهراس لنا فضربتها باسفله حتى تكسرت (ب) (سن للبیہقی ، باب من اراق مالا يحل الانتفاع به من الخمر وغيرها وكسر دعا کھا ، ج سادس، ص ۱۶۷، نمبر ۱۱۵۵۲) اس اثر میں شراب کا برتن تو ڑا گیا اور کوئی ضمان لازم نہیں کیا اس لئے حرام چیزوں کو توڑنے سے ضمان لازم نہیں ہوگا ۔

Book of Usurpation. [1575 (25)] And if a Muslim consumes the wine of a dhimmi or his pig, he is liable for its value. But if a Muslim consumes them from a Muslim, he is not liable. It is not so. [1575] (25) If a Muslim destroys the wine of a dhimmi or his pig, he will be guarantor for the value of both. And if a Muslim destroys both from a Muslim, he will not be guarantor. Because wine and pig are valuable items for the dhimmi, so when a Muslim destroys them, he must give their value. However, wine is of a standard type (dhawat al-amthal) but a Muslim cannot buy it, so he will pay its value. And pig is of a value type (dhawat al-qeem), so he will pay its value. And if a Muslim destroys the wine or pig of a Muslim, there is no liability for them. Because for a Muslim, both things are not valuable. Therefore, no liability is incumbent upon him for destroying them. (2) There is a hadith that these things were broken, rather he ordered to break them. From Salamah ibn Akwa' that the Prophet (peace be upon him) saw fires kindled on the day of Khaybar. He said: 'For what are these fires kindled?' They said: 'For the domestic donkeys.' He said: 'Break them and spill them.' (Bukhari, chapter on whether jars containing wine are broken or skins are burnt, p. 336 no. 2477) In this hadith, he ordered to throw away the meat of the donkey and break its vessel, from which it is known that there is no liability for breaking prohibited items. (3) From Anas ibn Malik: 'I was serving Abu 'Ubaydah, Abu Talhah, and Ubayy ibn Ka'b a drink made from unripe dates and ripe dates. Then a visitor came and said: 'Indeed wine has been made unlawful.' Abu Talhah said: 'O Anas, get up to these jars and break them.' Anas said: 'So I went to a mortar of ours and struck them with its bottom until they were broken.' (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, chapter on those who spill what it is not permissible to benefit from including wine and breaking its vessels, vol. 6, p. 167 no. 11552) In this athar, the wine vessel was broken and no liability was imposed. Therefore, breaking prohibited things does not entail liability. Footnote: (a) He saw the fire kindled on the day of Khaybar and said: 'For what are these fires kindled?' They said: 'For the domestic donkeys.' He said: 'Break the vessels and spill the meat.' (b) Anas ibn Malik says: 'I was serving Abu 'Ubaydah, Abu Talhah, and Ubayy ibn Ka'b wine made from dried and fresh dates. Then someone came and said that wine had become forbidden. Abu Talhah said: 'O Anas, go and break these waterskins.' Anas says: 'I went to a pickaxe and struck them with its bottom until I broke them.'
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Ghasb presents a ruling on liability for destroying wine or a pig belonging to a dhimmi (non-Muslim protected citizen) versus a Muslim. The author first states the ruling: if a Muslim destroys a dhimmi's wine or pig, he must pay their value; but if he destroys those of a Muslim, he is not liable. The commentary then explains the reasoning: for a dhimmi, wine and pig are valuable property (maal mutaqawwim) because they are permitted to use them. Wine is classified as 'dhawat al-amthal' (things of standard measure/type) but since a Muslim cannot buy it, he pays its value. Pig is 'dhawat al-qeem' (things of value), so he pays its value. For a Muslim, however, these are not valuable property at all, so destroying them incurs no liability. The author then provides two proofs: (1) The hadith of Salamah ibn Akwa' where the Prophet ordered to break the pots of donkey meat and spill the contents, indicating that destroying unlawful items does not necessitate liability. (2) The athar of Anas where Abu Talhah ordered him to break the wine jars after the prohibition of wine, and no compensation was demanded. Thus, the ruling is established that liability only attaches when the destroyed item is considered valuable property in the sight of its owner. Note: The Hanafi position is that a dhimmi's wine and pig are valuable property, so destroying them incurs liability. This is mufta bihi.

Key Terms
ضمان (damān): Liability or guarantee to compensate for loss; here, payment of value for destroyed property.
ذمی (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject living under Muslim rule with a covenant (dhimmah) granting protection.
قیمت (qīmah): Monetary value assessed by the market; here, the amount to be paid as compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

If a Muslim destroys the wine of a dhimmi, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) No liability because wine is impure
B) He must pay its value
C) He must only apologize
D) He must pay double its value

Key Takeaway
A Muslim is liable for destroying a dhimmi's wine or pig because these are valuable property for the dhimmi, even though they are prohibited for Muslims.

Definition and Ruling of Wadi'ah as a Trust Deposit
Definition of trust deposit and ruling that it is a trust with no liability unless trespass

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define wadi'ah, state its ruling as an amanah, and identify the condition for liability (ta'addi).

Core Idea
A deposit (wadi'ah) is a trust: the depositary is not liable unless he commits trespass or negligence.

This lesson defines wadi'ah (trust deposit) and establishes its fundamental ruling: it is an amanah in the hands of the depositary. The Qur'an and Sunnah confirm that the trustee is not liable for loss unless he exceeds proper limits. Understanding this principle is essential for grasping the Hanafi position on liability in gratuitous contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms
Learn the terms mudi' (depositor), mudda' (depositary), and wadi'ah (the deposited item), distinguishing their Arabic forms.
The vocalization (kasra/fatha) is important to avoid confusion.
2
Recognize the ruling
Understand that wadi'ah is an amanah (trust). The depositary is not liable for loss if it perishes without his fault.
The ruling is explicitly stated: 'al-wadi'ah amanah'.
3
Locate the proofs
Identify the Quranic verse (4:58) and hadith (Ibn Majah, Dar Qutni) as the primary dalils. Also note the athar of 'Umar ibn al-Khattab.
The hadith contains the operative phrase 'la daman' (no liability).
4
Understand the condition for liability
Liability only arises when the depositary commits ta'addi (trespass) or tafrit (negligence). The texts condition the ruling on the absence of fault.
The phrase 'ghayr al-mughall' means 'who does not commit fault'.
5
Note the practical implication
The depositary may keep the item himself or with his household; this does not create liability unless there is a breach of trust.
This is a permissive dispensation, not an obligation.

ثبوت اس آیت میں ہے۔ ان الله يأمركم ان تودوا الامانات إلى اهلها (الف) ( آیت ۵۸ سورۃ النساء۴ ) اس آیت میں امانت کو واپس لوٹانے کی تاکید کی گئی ہے۔ حدیث میں ہے۔ عن عمر بن شعيب عن ابيه عن جده قال قال رسول الله له من اودع وديعة فلا ضمان علیه (ب) (ابن ماجہ شریف، باب الوديعه ص ۳۴۳ نمبر ۲۴۰۱ / دار قطنی ، کتاب البیوع ج ثالث ص ۳۶ نمبر ۲۹۳۸) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ کوئی چیز امانت پر رکھے تو امانت رکھنے والے پر ضمان نہیں ہے۔ اس سے امانت کا ثبوت بھی ہوا اور اس کا حکم بھی معلوم ہوا کہ بغیر تعدی کے ہلاک ہونے پر ضمان نہیں ہے۔
اس باب میں تین الفاظ ہیں (۱) امانت رکھنے والا جس کو مودع کہتے ہیں ( دال کے کسرہ کے ساتھ ) (۲) جس کے پاس امانت رکھی جائے اس کو مودع کہتے ہیں ( دال کے فتحہ کے ساتھ اسم مفعول کا صیغہ ) (۳) جو چیز امانت رکھی جائے اس کو ودیعت کہتے ہیں۔ [۱۵۷۶] (۱) ودیعت امانت ہوتی ہے امانت رکھنے والے کے ہاتھ میں۔ اگر اس کے ہاتھ میں ہلاک ہو جائے تو اس کا ضامن نہیں ہوگا جس کے ہاتھ میں امانت رکھی گئی ہے وہ اس کے ہاتھ میں امانت ہے ۔ اور امانت کا قاعدہ یہ ہے کہ اگر بغیر تعدی کے ہلاک ہو جائے تو اس پر اس کا تاوان نہیں ہے۔ (1) ابن ماجہ کی حدیث اوپر گزری من اودع وديعة فلا ضمان عليه ابن ماجه شریف نمبر ۱ ۲۴۰) (۲) دار قطنی میں ہے عن عمرو بن شعيب عن ابيه عن جاءه عن النبي الله قال ليس على المستعير غير المغل ضمان ولا على المستودع غير المغل ضمان (ج) (دار قطنی، کتاب البيوع ج ثالث ص ۳۶ نمبر ۲۹۳۹) (۴) قال عمر بن الخطاب العارية بمنزلة الوديعة ولا ضمان فيها الا ان يتعدى (و) (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب العارية ، ج ثامن، ص ۷۹ انمبر ۱۴۷۸۵) اس حدیث اور اوپر کے اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ امانت بغیر تعدی کے ہلاک ہو جائے تو امین پر مضمان نہیں ہے۔
Book of Trusts. Book of Trusts. Al-Wadi'ah is a trust in the hand of the depositary. If it perishes in his hand, he is not liable for it. [1577](2) And for the depositary, he may keep it himself and with those in his household. Book of Trusts. Essential note: The meaning of wadi'ah is to place something as a trust. If someone places his goods with another for safekeeping, that is called placing a trust. Its proof is in this verse: 'Indeed, Allah commands you to render trusts to their owners' (Surah al-Nisa', 4:58). This verse emphasizes returning the trust. In the hadith: From 'Amr ibn Shu'ayb from his father from his grandfather said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever receives a deposit, there is no liability on him.' (Ibn Majah, chapter of wadi'ah, p. 343 no. 2401; Dar Qutni, book of sales, vol. 3, p. 36 no. 2938). This hadith states that if someone places a trust, there is no liability on the one holding the trust. From this, the proof of trust is established and its ruling is known: if it perishes without trespass, there is no liability. In this chapter there are three terms: (1) The one who places the trust is called 'mudi' (with kasra on the dal); (2) The one with whom the trust is placed is called 'mudda' (with fatha on the dal, formula of passive participle); (3) The item placed as trust is called 'wadi'ah'. [1576] (1) Wadi'ah is a trust in the hand of the depositary. If it perishes in his hand, the one in whose hand the trust is placed will not be liable. And the rule of trust is that if it perishes without trespass, there is no compensation on him. (1) The hadith of Ibn Majah passed earlier: 'Whoever receives a deposit, there is no liability on him.' (Ibn Majah no. 2401) (2) In Dar Qutni: from 'Amr ibn Shu'ayb from his father from his grandfather from the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'There is no liability on the borrower who is not at fault, nor on the depositary who is not at fault.' (Dar Qutni, book of sales, vol. 3, p. 36 no. 2939) (4) 'Umar ibn al-Khattab said: 'A loan (ariyah) is like a trust; there is no liability in it unless there is trespass.' (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, chapter of ariyah, vol. 8, p. 79 no. 14785) From this hadith and the previous athar, it is learned that if a trust perishes without trespass, there is no liability on the trustee (amin).
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage opens Kitab al-Wadi'ah (Book of Trusts). The author first defines wadi'ah: it is an amanah (trust) in the hand of the depositary (mudda'). The ruling: if the deposited item perishes in his hand without his fault (trespass/ta'addi), he is not liable. The author then provides proofs: (1) Quranic verse 4:58 commanding the return of trusts. (2) Hadith from Ibn Majah: 'Whoever receives a deposit, there is no liability on him.' (3) Hadith from Dar Qutni: 'No liability on the depositary who is not at fault.' (4) Athar from 'Umar ibn al-Khattab comparing ariyah (loan) to wadi'ah. The passage also clarifies the terminology: mudi' (depositor, with kasr on dal), mudda' (depositary, with fatha on dal), and wadi'ah (the deposited item). The core principle: a trustee (amin) is not liable for loss unless he commits trespass (ta'addi) or negligence (tafrit). This is a foundational rule in Hanafi fiqh for all gratuitous contracts (trusts).

Key Terms
وديعة (wadi'ah): The deposited item placed with another for safekeeping without compensation.
أمانة (amanah): A trust; an item held by someone who is not liable for its loss unless at fault.
تعدی (ta'addi): Trespass or wrongdoing; exceeding proper limits in handling the trust, such as using it improperly or keeping it unsafely.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Always, because it is his responsibility
B) Only if he commits trespass (ta'addi) or negligence
C) Only if the item is stolen
D) He is never liable

Key Takeaway
Wadi'ah is a trust; the depositary is not liable for loss without trespass or negligence.

Depositary may keep trust himself or with his household
Permission for depositary to safeguard the trust personally or via household

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the scope of the depositary's authority to delegate safekeeping of the deposit and identify who qualifies as 'ayal (household) according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The depositary may entrust the deposit to his household as if keeping it himself.

This is a straightforward ruling from the chapter on wadi'ah (trusts). It clarifies that the depositary is not required to keep the item personally; he may delegate to his own dependents. This is based on custom and avoids unnecessary hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the depositary's delegation of safekeeping to his household. The ruling is jā'iz (permissible).
2
Define 'ayal
'Ayal means the depositary's dependents: spouse, children, servants, and anyone he supports. They are considered trustworthy by default.
3
Distinguish household from outsiders
The depositary may not delegate to strangers without the owner's consent, as that would be a breach of trust (ta'dī).
4
Note the consequence of violation
If he entrusts to an outsider without permission, he becomes liable (dāmin) for any loss.
The deposit itself remains valid, but the depositary's action is considered trespass.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۵۷۷] (۲) امانت رکھنے والے کے لئے جائز ہے کہ خود حفاظت کرے اور ان سے حفاظت کرائے جو اس کے عیال میں ہیں ۔ تخری امانت کے مال کی خود حفاظت کر سکتا ہے اور ان لوگوں سے بھی حفاظت کروا سکتا ہے جو اس کے بال بچے ہیں یا جس کی وہ کفالت کرتا
[1577] (2) It is permissible for the depositary to safeguard it himself and to have it safeguarded by those who are in his household. Explanation: He can safeguard the trust property himself and can also have it safeguarded by those who are his children or whom he supports.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This is a short ruling following the definition of wadi'ah. It states that the depositary (the one holding the trust) has the authority to keep the deposited item himself or to have it kept by members of his household ('ayal). The commentary explains that 'household' includes his children and dependents. This is based on the customary practice that the depositary's family members are considered extensions of him in matters of safekeeping. The depositary does not need explicit permission from the owner to delegate to his own household, as they are deemed trustworthy by default. However, he may not entrust it to outsiders without permission, as that would be a form of trespass.

Key Terms
عیال ('ayal): Dependents; family members whom the depositary supports, such as spouse, children, servants.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The depositary's delegation is limited to his household; beyond that requires the owner's consent.

Definition of Wadi'ah and Its Ruling as an Amanah
Opening the chapter on deposit with definition and legal ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of wadi'ah, identify its ruling as an amanah, and explain why the depositary is not liable without transgression (ta'addi).

Core Idea
A deposit is a trust: the depositary is not liable for loss unless he transgresses.

This lesson opens the Book of Deposit (Kitab al-Wadi'ah) by defining the term and establishing its fundamental legal nature. We will examine the primary textual proofs from the Quran, Sunnah, and the saying of Umar ibn al-Khattab that together establish the principle of non-liability absent transgression.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
First, understand that wadi'ah is a contract where one entrusts property to another for safekeeping. The ruling is that it is a trust (amānah), meaning the depositary is not a guarantor.
Focus on the distinction between trust and guarantee.
2
Locate the primary dalil – Quran
The Quranic verse (Surah an-Nisa 4:58) commands rendering trusts to their owners. The deposit is a trust that must be returned. This establishes the obligation to return but does not directly address liability for loss.
The imperative (tu'addū) indicates obligation.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah of the hadith
The hadith 'Whoever places a deposit, there is no guarantee upon him' explicitly negates liability (daman) for the depositary. This proves that the deposit is a trust, and the depositary is not liable unless he transgresses.
The word 'fala daman' (no guarantee) is key.
4
Examine the supporting athar
Umar ibn al-Khattab said, 'The loan is like the deposit; there is no guarantee unless he transgresses.' This reinforces that both are trusts, and transgression triggers liability.
The athar provides early consensus.
5
Synthesize the ruling
Combine the proofs: the deposit is a trust; the depositary is not liable for loss without transgression. If the depositary commits ta'addi (e.g., uses the deposit without permission), he becomes a guarantor.
This is the foundational principle for the entire chapter.

حاشیہ : (الف) اللہ تم کو حکم دیتے ہیں کہ امانت امانت والوں کو ادا کرے (ب) آپ نے فرمایا جس نے امانت رکھی اس پر ضمان نہیں ہے (ج) آپ نے فرمایا عاریت رکھنے والے پر جو زیادتی نہ کرتا ہو ضمان نہیں ہے، اور امانت رکھنے والے پر جو زیادتی نہ کرتا ہو ضمان نہیں ہے (1) حضرت عمر بن خطاب نے فرمایا عاریت ودیعت کی طرح ہے اور اس میں ضمان نہیں ہے مگر یہ کہ تعدی کرے۔
Book of Deposit. (Necessary note) The meaning of deposit is to entrust for safekeeping. If someone places his item with another for safekeeping, it is called entrusting. Its proof is in this verse: 'Indeed, Allah commands you to render trusts to their owners' (Surah an-Nisa 4:58). This verse emphasizes returning trusts. In the hadith: From Amr ibn Shu'ayb from his father from his grandfather that the Messenger of Allah ﷺ said: 'Whoever places a deposit, there is no guarantee upon him.' (Ibn Majah, Bab al-Wadi'ah, p. 343 no. 2401; Dar Qutni, Kitab al-Buyu', vol. 3, p. 36 no. 2938). This hadith indicates that if someone places an item as a trust, the depositary is not liable. From this, the ruling is established that without transgression, if it perishes, there is no guarantee. In this chapter, there are three terms: (1) The one who deposits, called al-mudi' (with kasra on dal); (2) The one with whom the deposit is placed, called al-mudi' (with fatha on dal, passive participle); (3) The deposited item, called al-wadi'ah. [1576] (1) The deposit is a trust in the hand of the depositary. If it perishes in his hand, he will not be a guarantor. The one in whose hand the deposit is placed, it is a trust in his hand. The principle of trust is that if it perishes without transgression, there is no compensation for it. (1) The hadith of Ibn Majah passed above: 'Whoever places a deposit, there is no guarantee upon him.' Ibn Majah no. 2401. (2) In Dar Qutni: From Amr ibn Shu'ayb from his father from his grandfather [؟] from the Prophet ﷺ: 'There is no guarantee upon the borrower who is not negligent, and no guarantee upon the depositary who is not negligent.' (Dar Qutni, Kitab al-Buyu', vol. 3, p. 36 no. 2939). (4) Umar ibn al-Khattab said: 'The loan is like the deposit; there is no guarantee in it unless he transgresses.' (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, Bab al-Ariyah, vol. 8, p. 79 no. 14785). From this hadith and the above athar, it is understood that if the trust perishes without transgression, the trustee is not liable. Footnote: (الف) Allah commands you to render trusts to their owners. (ب) The Prophet said: Whoever places a deposit, there is no guarantee upon him. (ج) The Prophet said: There is no guarantee upon the borrower who is not negligent, and no guarantee upon the depositary who is not negligent. (1) Umar ibn al-Khattab said: The loan is like the deposit, and there is no guarantee in it unless one transgresses.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Deposit (Kitab al-Wadi'ah) in the Sharh al-Thamiri. The author first gives a necessary note defining the term 'wadi'ah' as entrusting an item to someone for safekeeping. He then provides the legal proof from the Quran (Surah an-Nisa 4:58) which commands the rendering of trusts to their owners, establishing the obligation to return the deposit. The primary hadith evidence is from Ibn Majah and Dar Qutni where the Prophet ﷺ explicitly states that there is no guarantee (damān) upon one who receives a deposit, meaning he is not liable for loss unless he transgresses. The passage also clarifies the three terminological distinctions: al-mudi' (depositor, with kasra) and al-mudi' (depositary, with fatha), and al-wadi'ah (the deposited item). The core ruling is stated: the deposit is a trust (amānah) in the depositary's hand; if it perishes without any transgression (ta'addī), the depositary is not liable. This is reinforced by the hadith about the borrower and depositary, and the athar of Umar ibn al-Khattab equating the loan (ariyah) with the deposit (wadi'ah) in terms of non-liability absent transgression. The footnote explains the Arabic texts of the proofs. This establishes the fundamental principle of the chapter: a deposit is a trust, not a guarantee, and the depositary's liability only arises through transgression or negligence.

Key Terms
الوديعة (al-wadi'ah): The property that is entrusted to another for safekeeping.
المودع (بكسر الدال) (al-mūdi' (with kasra)): The depositor, i.e., the owner who places the deposit.
المودع (بفتح الدال) (al-mūdi' (with fatha)): The depositary, i.e., the person who receives the deposit for safekeeping.
ضمان (daman): Liability, guarantee; the obligation to compensate for loss or damage.
تعدي (ta'addi): Transgression; any act that goes beyond the permitted limits of custody, such as misuse or mishandling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, under what condition does a depositary (mustawda') become liable for a lost deposit?
A) If the deposit is stolen from him
B) If the deposit perishes in his hand without any fault on his part
C) If he commits transgression (ta'addi) in handling the deposit
D) If he fails to return the deposit on demand

Key Takeaway
A deposit is a trust; the depositary is not liable for accidental loss unless he commits ta'addi (transgression).

Depositary May Keep the Deposit with His Family
Ruling with proof on deposit safekeeping

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the depositary's permission to entrust the deposit to his family and explain the proof from the Prophet’s practice.

Core Idea
The depositary may entrust the deposit to his family; necessity and prophetic practice permit it.

This passage addresses a practical question: must the depositary always keep the deposit personally, or can he delegate to his family? The Hanafi school, based on common need and a clear precedent from the Prophet ﷺ, holds that family members (ʿiyāl) are trustworthy custodians. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its justification, and the underlying proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Pinpoint the precise act being ruled: the depositary (mustawdaʿ) delegating safekeeping of the deposit to his family members. Determine the hukm level – here, it is permissible (mubah).
The passage states 'jāʾiz' (permissible), not obligatory.
2
Locate the dalīl
Find the proof cited: the hadith of ʿĀʾisha about the Hijrah, where the Prophet ﷺ ordered ʿAlī to stay behind in Mecca to return the deposits. Note that this is a sunnah aḥad (solitary hadith) with zannī transmission and indication.
The hadith is from Sunan al-Bayhaqī.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalālah
Explain how the hadith proves the ruling: the Prophet entrusted the deposits to ʿAlī, who was from his household (ʿiyāl). This shows that entrusting deposits to family members is acceptable and does not impose liability.
ʿAlī is considered from the Prophet's family (ahl al-bayt).
4
Note the ikhtilāf
Some scholars (e.g., the Shāfiʿī school) may restrict the depositary to keeping the deposit only himself, arguing that delegation is a form of negligence unless specifically allowed. The Hanafī position is the muftā bihī.
The source labels this as mukhtalaf fīh.
5
Evaluate the Hanafī tarjīḥ
The Hanafī school relies on both practical necessity (a person cannot always be home) and the prophetic precedent. This combines istiḥsān with dalīl naqlī to support the ruling.
No explicit tarjīḥ authority is given, but the ruling is from Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah.

[۱۵۷۷] (۲) امانت رکھنے والے کے لئے جائز ہے کہ خود حفاظت کرے اور ان سے حفاظت کرائے جو اس کے عیال میں ہیں ۔ تخری امانت کے مال کی خود حفاظت کر سکتا ہے اور ان لوگوں سے بھی حفاظت کروا سکتا ہے جو اس کے بال بچے ہیں یا جس کی وہ کفالت کرتا ہے۔ اے آدمی ہر وقت گھر میں نہیں رہتا، کبھی باہر بھی جانا پڑتا ہے۔ اس لئے اگر اپنے اہل وعیال سے امانت کی حفاظت نہ کرائے گا تو کس سے کرائے گا؟ (۲) حضور نے ہجرت کے موقع پر حضرت علی سے امانت کی حفاظت کروائی ہے۔ بلکہ انہیں کے ذریعہ امانت ادا کروائی ہے۔ عن عائشة في هجرة النبي الا الله قالت امر تعنى رسول الله الا الله عليا ان يتخلف عنه بمكة حتى يؤدى عن رسول الله الا الله الودائع التي كانت عنده للناس (الف) (سن الليبقى ، باب ما جاء في الترغیب فی اداء الامانات ، ج سادس جس ۴۷۲ نمبر ۱۲۶۹۶) اس حدیث میں حضرت علی نے حضور کی امانت کی حفاظت کی اور اس کو لوگوں کو ادا بھی کیا اور حضرت علی آپ کے عیال میں سے تھے۔ اس لئے اہل وعیال سے بھی حفاظت کروا سکتا ہے۔
حاشیہ : (الف) حضور کی ہجرت کے بارے میں حضرت عائشہ فرماتی ہیں کہ حضور نے حضرت علی کو حکم دیا کہ وہ مکہ مکرمہ میں ان کی نیابت کریں۔ یہاں تک کہ حضور کی امانت کو ادا کر دیں جو ان کے پاس لوگوں کی ہے
[1577] (2) It is permissible for the depositary to keep it himself and to entrust its protection to those who are in his family (or dependents). He can protect the deposit himself, and he can also entrust its protection to his children or those he supports. A person is not always at home; sometimes he has to go out. Therefore, if he does not entrust protection to his family, then to whom will he entrust? (2) The Prophet ﷺ at the time of Hijrah entrusted the protection of deposits to Ali by saying that Ali stay behind in Mecca until he delivers the deposits that were with the Prophet for the people. In this hadith, Ali protected the Prophet's deposits and delivered them, and Ali was among the Prophet's family. Hence, one can entrust protection to family members.
Footnote: (الف) Aisha says about the Prophet's Hijrah: The Prophet ordered Ali to stay behind in Mecca to act as his deputy, until he delivers the trusts that were with the Prophet belonging to the people.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage presents the second ruling of the chapter: the depositary (mustawda') may choose to keep the deposit himself or delegate its protection to his family members (those in his charge, such as his wife, children, or dependents). The justification is practical: a person cannot be home all the time, and it is natural to rely on one's household for safekeeping. The proof is the practice of the Prophet ﷺ during the Hijrah when he entrusted his deposits to Ali ibn Abi Talib, who was part of his household. This indicates that entrusting the deposit to family members is permissible and does not make the depositary liable if the deposit is lost without transgression.

Key Terms
عيال (iyāl): Family members whom one is responsible for supporting, such as spouse, children, and dependents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Only with himself, no one else
B) With any trustworthy person
C) With his family members (ʿiyāl) only
D) With a professional trustee

Key Takeaway
The Ḥanafī school permits depositors to delegate safekeeping of a deposit to their family members, based on necessity and the Prophet’s practice.

Liability for Sub-Depositing: Rule and Emergency Exception
Ruling on liability when depositary sub-deposits to non-family

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that sub-depositing to non-family without permission makes the depositary liable (damin) and identify the two emergency exceptions (fire, sinking ship) where liability is avoided.

Core Idea
Sub-depositing without permission makes the depositary liable; emergencies like fire or sinking ship exempt him.

In the law of deposit (wadi'ah), the depositary must keep the property himself or with his household. If he gives it to a stranger without the owner's permission, he becomes liable for any loss. But what if an emergency forces him to move the deposit? This lesson explores the rule and its exceptions, citing the athar of Qadi Shuraih as the primary evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default liability
The depositary (mustawda') sub-deposits the property to someone outside his family (ghayr ahl wa iyal). According to the text, this act triggers liability (daman) for the depositary.
The owner is deemed to have consented only to the depositary's own custody or that of his household.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the athar of Qadi Shuraih: 'Whoever receives a deposit and then sub-deposits it without the owner's permission, he becomes a guarantor.' This is a qawl sahabi (saying of a Companion) recorded in Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq.
This athar is considered zanni al-thubut but is relied upon in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit phrase 'without the owner's permission' shows that unauthorized sub-deposit creates liability. The Hanafi school applies the principle: al-amr yufidu al-wujub? Here it's a direct statement of consequence (daman).
The athar is a clear textual indicator (nass) of liability.
4
Identify the exceptions
The passage lists two emergencies: (1) fire in the depositary's house, where he may throw the deposit to a neighbor; (2) fear of a ship sinking, where he may transfer it to another ship. In these cases, the depositary does not become liable.
The underlying principle is necessity (idṭirār) and the intention to preserve the deposit.
5
Apply the principle to new cases
Any similar emergency where the depositary acts for preservation can be analogized. The depositary must exercise reasonable care. If the deposit is lost despite his precaution, he remains a trustee, not a guarantor.
The exception is limited to genuine emergencies; mere convenience does not excuse liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۵۷۸] (۳) پس اگر اس کی حفاظت کروائی اہل و عیال کے علاوہ سے تو ضامن ہو جائے گا مگر یہ کہ اس کے گھر میں آگ لگ جائے تو اس کو اپنے پڑوسی کے سپر د کر دے یا امانت کشتی میں ہو اور اس کو ڈوبنے کا خوف ہو تو اس کو دوسری کشتی میں ڈال دے۔
امانت کی چیز کو اپنے اہل وعیال کے علاوہ سے حفاظت کراوئی تو مودع اس کا ضامن بن جائے گا۔ مالک مودع کی حفاظت سے راضی ہے اس کے علاوہ کی حفاظت سے راضی نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے دوسروں سے حفاظت کروائی اور ہلاک ہو گئی تو ضامن ہوگا ۔ اس اثر میں ہے عن شريح قال من استودع وديعة فاستودعهـا بـغـيــر اذن اهلها فقد ضمن (ب) ( مصنف عبدالرزاق ، باب الوديعة ، ج ثامن ص ۱۸۲، نمبر ۱۴۸۰۰) اس اثر میں ہے کہ مالک کی اجازت کے بغیر امین نے دوسرے کے پاس امانت رکھی اور ہلاک ہوئی تو وہ ضامن ہوگا۔ البتہ ایسی مجبوری ہو جائے کہ پڑوسی کو دینا ضروری ہو جائے اور اس کو دینے میں حفاظت کا زیادہ امکان ہوتو مودع ضامن نہیں ہوگا۔ مثلا گھر میں آگ لگ گئی ایسے موقع پر امانت کی چیز حفاظت کی نیت سے پڑوسی کے گھر میں پھینک دی اور امانت ضائع ہو گئی تو مودع ضامن نہیں ہوگا۔ اسی طرح امانت کی چیز کشتی میں تھی اور اندیشہ ہوا کہ وہ چیز ڈوب جائے گی جس کی وجہ سے حفاظت کی نیت سے دوسری کشتی میں ڈال دی اور وہ ہلاک ہو گئی تو مودع ضامن نہیں ہو گا ۔ اصول یہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ مجبوری ہو اور حفاظت کی غرض سے عیال کے علاوہ سے حفاظت کروائی تو مودع ضامن نہیں ہوگا۔ حریق : آگ لگنا، سفیدی : کشتی۔
حاشیہ : (ب) قاضی شریح نے فرمایا کسی نے امانت رکھی پس اس نے مالک کی اجازت کے بغیر دوسرے کے پاس امانت رکھ دی تو وہ ضامن ہو جائے گا۔
[1578] (3) Then if he entrusts its protection to other than his family, he becomes a guarantor, unless a fire occurs in his house so he hands it over to his neighbor, or if it is on a ship and he fears drowning so he throws it onto another ship. If the depositary entrusts the deposit to someone other than his family, he becomes a guarantor. The owner is satisfied with the depositary's own protection, not with others. Therefore, if he entrusts it to others and it perishes, he will be a guarantor. In this athar: from Shuraih: 'Whoever receives a deposit and then sub-deposits it without the owner's permission, he becomes a guarantor.' (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, Bab al-Wadi'ah, vol. 8, p. 182, no. 14800). This athar indicates that if the trustee deposits with another without the owner's permission and it perishes, he is liable. However, if there is a necessity, such as giving to a neighbor becomes necessary and there is a greater chance of protection, then the depositary will not be liable. For example, if a fire breaks out in the house and he throws the deposit to the neighbor's house for protection and it is lost, the depositary is not liable. Similarly, if the deposit is on a ship and he fears it will sink, so he puts it on another ship for protection and it is lost, the depositary is not liable. The principle is that if there is a necessity and he entrusts to others for protection, the depositary is not liable. Hariq means fire; safini means ship.
Footnote: (ب) Qadi Shuraih said: Someone placed a deposit, and then he sub-deposited it with another without the owner's permission; so he becomes a guarantor.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage presents the third ruling: if the depositary (mustawda') gives the deposit to someone outside his family (i.e., not his dependents), he becomes liable (damin) for its loss. The reasoning is that the owner consented only to the depositary's own custody or that of his household. Without such permission, sub-depositing is a breach of trust. The proof is the athar of Qadi Shuraih, who explicitly states that sub-depositing without the owner's permission results in liability. However, the law makes an exception for emergencies: if the depositary's house catches fire, he may throw the deposit to a neighbor for safety; if he is on a ship and fears it will sink, he may transfer it to another ship. In such cases, his action is aimed at preservation, not negligence, so he remains a trustee and is not liable if the deposit is lost. The passage concludes with the principle that necessity (idṭirār) for the sake of preservation overrides the general prohibition on sub-depositing.

Key Terms
ضامن (dāmin): One who is liable to pay compensation for loss or damage.
حریق (ḥarīq): Fire; a situation that necessitates moving the deposit for safety.
سفينة (safīnah): Ship; the context is fear of drowning leading to transfer of deposit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which of the following scenarios does the depositary (mustawda') NOT become liable (dāmin) for sub-depositing?
A) He gives the deposit to his neighbor for no reason
B) He gives the deposit to his brother for safekeeping
C) He throws the deposit to a neighbor's house when his house is on fire
D) He leaves the deposit with a stranger without the owner's permission

Key Takeaway
Sub-depositing without permission triggers liability; emergencies like fire or sinking ship are exceptions where liability is waived.

Mixing Deposit with Own Property Indistinguishably Results in Guarantee
Ruling on mixing deposit with depositary's own property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mixing a deposit indistinguishably with the depositary’s own property and identify the key condition that triggers liability.

Core Idea
Mixing a deposit beyond recognition transforms the depositary from trustee to guarantor.

When a depositary mixes a deposited item with his own belongings in a way that the deposit can no longer be separately identified, Hanafi law holds him liable for the deposit's value. This rule protects the owner's right to the exact deposit and ensures that trust property is not compromised. In this lesson, you will examine the precise condition that triggers this liability and the consequences of such mixing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the depositary mixing the deposit with his own property. The ruling is that he becomes a guarantor (dāmin), i.e., liable to compensate if lost.
2
Isolate the critical condition
The condition is that the mixture becomes indistinguishable (lā yatamayyaz). If the deposit can still be identified separately, no liability arises.
The Arabic phrase 'خلطا لا يتميز' explicitly requires indistinguishability.
3
Understand the rationale
By making the deposit indistinguishable, the depositary destroys its specific identity, which is a form of transgression (ta'addī). This breach of trust triggers liability.
4
Determine the consequence
If the mixed property is lost, the depositary must compensate the owner for the full value of the deposit. The liability is fixed at the moment of mixing.
5
Contrast with distinguishable mixing
If the deposit can still be identified after mixing (e.g., different colored grains), the deposit remains a trust (amānah) and no liability attaches to the depositary.
This distinction is crucial for applying the rule correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان اودعها مع ماله خلطا لا يتميز ضمن
[۱۵۷۹] (۴) اگر امانت رکھنے والے نے اپنے مال کے ساتھ ایسے ملادی کہ الگ نہ ہو سکتی ہو تو اس کا ضامن ہوگا۔
And if he deposits it with his property in a mixture that is indistinguishable, he becomes a guarantor. [1579] (4) If the depositary mixes the deposit with his own property such that it cannot be separated, then he becomes its guarantor.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage gives the fourth ruling: if the depositary (mustawda') mixes the deposited item with his own property in such a way that they cannot be distinguished (e.g., mixing money or grains), he becomes liable (dāmin) for the deposit. The reasoning is that by making the deposit indistinguishable, he destroys the specific identity of the trust, which amounts to transgression (ta'addī). Consequently, if the mixed property is lost, he must compensate the owner. This rule protects the owner's right to the exact deposit.

Key Terms
خلط (khalṭ): Mixing the deposit with the depositary's own property such that the deposit cannot be separately identified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the depositary become liable (dāmin) by mixing the deposit with his own property?
A) When he mixes it intentionally regardless of distinguishability
B) When he mixes it and the mixture becomes indistinguishable (lā yatamayyaz)
C) When he mixes it but the deposit can still be identified
D) When he mixes it and then loses it accidentally

Key Takeaway
A depositary becomes liable for the deposit if he mixes it with his own property in a way that cannot be distinguished, because such mixing destroys the specific identity of the trust.
